<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03715_figures/2605.03715_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 5. To simulate dissipative dynamics with a quantum computer, one can Trotterise reversible unitary circuits with QITE for the Lindbladian dissipator D[l]:(adapted from ref. [45]). Caveats: (i) the number of qubits doubles, (ii) long-time evolution is needed while keeping error low: deep circuits are needed, (iii) non-unitary evolution is implemented with QITE, so the superkets remain normalised, and (iv) initial states must be valid density operators of trace 1.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a method to use real-time Krylov subspace diagonalization to simulate open quantum systems. It successfully applies this technique to estimate the Liouvillian gap in Kerr cat qubits, demonstrating that dissipative evolution can be used to filter out unwanted modes.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a NISQ-friendly, unbiased alternative to QPE for studying dissipative dynamics and the emergence of protected subspaces in driven-dissipative systems, which is crucial for developing error-protected cat qubits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Extending real-time Quantum Subspace Diagonalisation (QSD) methods from closed Hamiltonian systems to open quantum systems described by Lindblad master equations, specifically addressing numerical instabilities caused by non-normality.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that QSD can faithfully reconstruct the Liouvillian gap and Wigner representations in the Kerr Cat qubit regime, showing that dissipative dynamics can act as a spectral filter to suppress fast-decaying modes.

**Method.** Real-time Krylov Subspace Diagonalisation (QSD) using a Generalized Eigenvalue Problem (GEVP) approach applied to superkets, implemented via Trotterization and Quantum Imaginary Time Evolution (QITE).

**Model / system.** A two-photon driven superconducting Kerr resonator in the Kerr Cat qubit regime, modeled by a Lindblad master equation featuring Kerr nonlinearity, detuning, and single-photon loss.

**Key observables.** Liouvillian gap, photon number, field amplitude expectation value, and Wigner representations of eigenstates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Kerr nonlinearity (K), two-photon pump strength (g), detuning (delta), photon loss rate (kappa), and Krylov dimension (D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method requires doubling the quantum register size to encode density operators, and the implementation assumes the ability to perform deep circuits and frequent trace estimations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 5 illustrates the quantum circuit architecture for simulating dissipative dynamics using Trotterized unitary circuits combined with QITE.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the extension of QSD to Lindblad dynamics, discusses the mathematical challenges of non-normality, applies the method to the Kerr Cat qubit model, and evaluates the accuracy and circuit implementation of the algorithm.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this chapter we present how real-time quantum subspace methods can be modified to simulate open quantum systems in Lindblad form. We apply these methods to a two-photon driven superconducting Kerr resonator and show how they allow to estimate the Liouvillian gap in the Kerr Cat qubit regime.   This is based on work done between November 2025 and January 2026 and on the talk given in early February 2026 at the Kwekfest 2026 conference, to celebrate L.C. Kwek's lifetime achievements.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.02995"></a>
### [Page Curve for Local-Operator Entanglement from Free Probability](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.02995v1)

**Authors:** Neil Dowling, Silvia Pappalardi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.02995v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.02995_figures/2605.02995_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1. The LOE Page Curve for N = 8 qubits as a function of subsystem size NA [blue, circles], alongside the maximum possible entropy [red, dotted]. At the half-chain bipartition, the correction to maximum is given by the state Page correc- tion of 1/2 plus an exponentially small (in N) value dependent on the fourth free-cumulant of O, κ4(O). Inset: the differ- ence between the (annealed) average 2-Rényi entanglement entropies of states (in doubled Hilbert space) versus opera- tors, for NA = N ¯ A.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.02995_figures/2605.02995_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. The non-crossing partition lattice for k = 4. Each node corresponds to a permutation, written below in stan- dard cyclic notation, while diagrammatically the isomorphic partition is displayed. We can identify the non-crossing par- tition subset of permutations corresponding to diagrams that do not cross; cf. the crossing partition depicted in the top right. Each line identifies unit distance according to the Cay- ley metric [Eq. (30)], while moving along a wire up the dia- gram corresponds to the (partial) ordering π ≤σ [Eq. (12)]. In purple [left] and green [right] are the geodesics σ ≤τγ and σ ≤τe.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper uses free probability to analytically derive the Page curve for Local-Operator Entanglement in chaotic systems. It demonstrates that the leading-order entanglement of operators is independent of the initial operator's higher-order correlations, depending only on two-point functions. This suggests that the long-time behavior of LOE is governed by standard ETH rather than the full ETH.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a rigorous link between operator scrambling, chaos, and the statistical properties of entanglement, suggesting that higher-order ETH structure is not required to describe LOE.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the Page curve for Local-Operator Entanglement (LOE) in chaotic systems and investigating whether its long-time behavior depends on higher-order correlations (full ETH) or only on two-point correlations (standard ETH).

**Main result.** The authors analytically show that the LOE asymptotically reproduces the Page curve for random states for traceless operators, and that the leading-order LOE is independent of the initial operator, depending only on its autocorrelation functions.

**Method.** The study employs free probability theory, Weingarten calculus, and the Brauer algebra to compute Rényi entropies and purities in the large-dimension limit.

**Model / system.** The system consists of many-body chaotic quantum systems undergoing Haar-random unitary dynamics, analyzed via a spatial bipartition of a D-dimensional Hilbert space.

**Key observables.** Local-Operator Entanglement (LOE), k-Rényi entanglement entropy, operator k-purity, and free cumulants.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Large-dimension limit (D >> 1), Rényi indices (k), subsystem size (NA), and the distinction between traceless and finite-trace operators.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The results are primarily valid in the large-D asymptotic limit, assuming Haar-random dynamics and self-averaging of the entanglement properties.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the LOE Page curve for 8 qubits compared to the maximum possible entropy; Figure 2 illustrates the non-crossing partition lattice for k=4, showing the geometric structure of permutations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LOE concept, applies free probability and Weingarten calculus to derive the Page curve, compares operator entanglement to state entanglement, and discusses implications for the Eigenstate Thermalization Hypothesis (ETH).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The local-operator entanglement (LOE) measures the classical simulability of a Heisenberg operator and is conjectured to witness many-body chaos in locally interacting systems. Using tools from free probability, we analytically compute its value for Haar random dynamics for all Rényi indices. We find that it asymptotically reproduces the Page curve for random states in the case of traceless operators, with exponentially deviating corrections. In contrast to higher-order out-of-time ordered correlators, which depend on operator correlations via free cumulants, the leading-order LOE is independent of the initial operator. Guided by our Haar result, we therefore argue that the long-time value of the LOE entropies in chaotic systems will depend only on autocorrelation functions of the initial operator up to exponentially small corrections, suggesting that the higher-order structure of the full Eigenstate Thermalization Hypothesis is not necessary to describe it.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03325"></a>
### [Time-boundary scattering and topological resonant transmissions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03325v1)

**Authors:** Haiping Hu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03325v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03325_figures/2605.03325_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Spatial versus temporal scattering. (a) In real space, a plane wave of fixed energy impinges on a potential V (x), leading to re- flected and transmitted waves and possibly bound states. The pro- cess is governed by stationary scattering theory. (b) In time, the Hamiltonian changes from HL to HR across a time boundary (TB). Bloch waves of definite crystal momentum act as scattering chan- nels, while energy is not conserved. A TB of duration τB generates a unitary evolution UB and the scattering obeys the Schr¨odinger equa- tion. Arrows pointing towards (away from) the TB indicate incoming (outgoing) waves, and colors distinguish states before and after the boundary.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03325_figures/2605.03325_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Resonant transmission (RT) across a TB for the 2D model in Eq. (7). (a) Schematic of the TB with time-varying mass terms mx(t) and mz(t). Band structures on either side are shown in blue and purple. (b) Trajectory of the poles of the S-matrix in the Bril- louin zone (BZ) as the TB duration τB increases (indicated by the arrow). Contour plots show the inter-channel transmission T−+ for two different τB values. The brightest points mark the RT. (c) Time evolution on the Bloch sphere for the RT mode (red) and an off- resonant mode k = (0.5, 0) (black). The system starts in the ground state of HL (black stars). The evolution inside and after the TB is shown by the solid and dashed...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03325_figures/2605.03325_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Topological view of RT in the elementary Clifford represen- tation. (a) For integer AZ classes in dD, the topological invariant is the mapping degree from the Brillouin zone (T d) to the d sphere (Sd) spanned by the normalized Hamiltonian vector ⃗h. A RT arises at a quenched TB when ⃗hL and ⃗hR are antiparallel. (b) Stability of RTs in even dimensions. Temporal modulations inside the TB can stretch the mapping encoded by the effective Hamiltonian Heff(k, t), but cannot alter the global topology without gap closings.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03325_figures/2605.03325_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 5. RT in the extended SSH model (15) and dynamical symmetry breaking. (a) Phase diagram with topologically distinct phases (col- ored regions) labeled by the winding number w1. Arrows indicate the three quenched TBs. (b) Inter-channel transmission probability T−+ versus momentum k for different TB settings. The blue, or- ange, and gray lines correspond to the quenched protocols 1⃝2⃝and 3⃝in (a). The red and dashed red lines are for temporal modulation HT B(t) = σz and HT B(t) = (1 −t/τB)HL + t/τBHR + σz, respectively.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a unified scattering theory for quantum systems undergoing temporal transitions. It identifies 'topological resonant transmissions' that allow perfect state transfer between bands and proves that the number of these resonances is protected by the change in the system's bulk topology.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful new tool for engineering and probing quantum dynamics, specifically offering a way to identify topological phases in cold atom systems by observing scattering patterns at high-symmetry points.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified scattering framework for quantum systems at time-boundaries (TBs), which are temporal analogues of spatial interfaces.

**Main result.** The development of a Bloch-wave scattering theory for TBs, establishing a bulk-time-boundary correspondence where the number of topological resonant transmissions (RTs) equals the jump in the bulk topological invariant.

**Method.** The authors introduce a temporal scattering matrix (S-matrix) and transfer matrix (M-matrix) approach, treating the time-varying region as a scattering interface between two static phases.

**Model / system.** A d-dimensional lattice system with a piecewise time-dependent Hamiltonian H(t) that transitions between two static bulk phases (H_L and H_R) over a duration tau_B.

**Key observables.** S-matrix elements (reflection/transmission amplitudes), inter-channel transmission probability, birefringence, and dynamical freezing of the quantum state.

**Important parameters / regimes.** TB duration (tau_B), topological invariants (Chern number, winding number), and Altland-Zirnbauer symmetry classes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework focuses on integer Altland-Zirnbauer classes and assumes a gap separating valence and conduction bands.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares spatial and temporal scattering; Figure 4 shows the effect of disorder on transmission; Figure 5 illustrates the impact of symmetry-breaking temporal modulation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of time-boundaries, develops the Bloch-wave scattering theory, establishes the bulk-time-boundary correspondence, analyzes dimensional dependence and robustness, and extends the analysis to higher-order poles and 3D systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Time boundaries (TBs), temporal analogues of spatial interfaces, offer a powerful handle to engineer quantum systems. However, unlike the well-developed stationary scattering theory at spatial interfaces, a unified framework for quantum scattering at TBs has been missing. Here we develop a Bloch-wave scattering theory for TBs by introducing a temporal scattering matrix $S$ between incoming and outgoing Bloch channels. We uncover topological resonant transmissions (RTs) -- poles of $S$ that yield perfect interband transmission and dynamical freezing of the quantum state. We establish a bulk-time-boundary correspondence for all integer Altland-Zirnbauer classes: the number of RTs equals the jump of the bulk topological invariant across the TB. In one dimension this gives a time-domain Levinson's theorem. A topological analysis further reveals a striking dimensional dependence. In even dimensions RTs are robust to temporal modulations and disorder, whereas in odd dimensions they can be destroyed by dynamical symmetry breaking. Our work places temporal and spatial scattering on the same footing and opens new avenues for engineering and probing quantum dynamics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03513"></a>
### [Unified Framework for Quantum Resource Recycling via Instrument-Dependent Back-action](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03513v1)

**Authors:** Zinuo Cai, Jianxin Song, Changliang Ren  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03513v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03513_figures/2605.03513_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Instrument-dependent measurement backaction. (a) Ellipti- cal reduction model. Coherent superposition of measurement chan- nels (L¨uders decomposition) constrains the transverse coherence re- duction factor FI to an elliptical trajectory dictated by sharpness α and bias β. (b) Linear reduction model. An incoherent probabilis- tic mixture (Σ) of completely dephasing (P±) and non-disturbing (I) branches yields a linear scaling FII. The elliptical mode strictly pro- vides greater backaction suppression (FI ≥FII).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03513_figures/2605.03513_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Correlation sharing boundaries in the bilateral sequential sce- nario. The blue dotted curve illustrates the Pareto front (maximum S2 for fixed S1) under generalized measurements, derived via global optimization over 100 discrete samples with specific optimal con- figurations. Gray dashed lines denote the classical bound (S = 2), while the light-gray shaded region highlights simultaneous CHSH violations (S1, S2 &gt; 2). Upper (lower) panels correspond to the el- liptical (linear) reduction models.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a unified framework to explain how the internal structure of quantum measurements determines the ability to reuse quantum correlations. It proves that Bell nonlocality can be shared among an unlimited number of sequential observers by tailoring measurement backaction. The study demonstrates that quantum instruments, not just measurement statistics, are the fundamental constraints on quantum resource recycling.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it provides a fundamental physical mechanism (instrument-dependent backaction) for the degradation of quantum resources during sequential measurements.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the fundamental limits of recycling quantum correlations, such as Bell nonlocality, in sequential multi-observer measurement scenarios.

**Main result.** The authors establish a unified framework showing that correlation sharing is governed by quantum-instrument-dependent backaction rather than just POVM statistics, proving that both unilateral and bilateral Bell nonlocality can be shared across arbitrary numbers of observers.

**Method.** The study uses a Kraus-decomposition-based functional framework to analyze measurement backaction, employing analytical derivations, small-angle limit analysis, and MultiStart Sequential Quadratic Programming (SQP) for optimization.

**Model / system.** The framework considers a single-qubit binary measurement observable and a pure entangled two-qubit state, evaluating two measurement reduction models: the Elliptical Reduction Model (based on Lüders decomposition) and the Linear Reduction Model.

**Key observables.** CHSH violation values (S-parameters), Bell nonlocality, and coherence retention (reduction factor F).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement sharpness (alpha), outcome bias (beta), measurement angles (phi, theta), and the number of sequential observers (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes specific measurement ansatzes, such as the elliptical and linear reduction models, and relies on the small-angle limit (phi to 0) for asymptotic scaling proofs.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates how the elliptical reduction model constrains coherence reduction to an elliptical trajectory; Figure 2 shows the Pareto frontier of CHSH violations in the bilateral scenario.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of correlation recycling, develops a unified quantum-instrument framework, analyzes unilateral sharing limits, explores bilateral sharing scenarios via optimization, and provides asymptotic scaling proofs for infinite sharing.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Accurate characterization of measurement backaction is crucial for understanding the limits of reusing quantum correlations in sequential scenarios. Here, we develop a unified quantum-instrument framework that goes beyond simple measurement statistics, explicitly attributing correlation sharing to Kraus-structure-dependent backaction. By tracing operational differences to this underlying physical mechanism, our framework integrates previously disparate strategies. Within this formulation, we derive general conditions for unbounded unilateral nonlocality sharing across arbitrarily many observers. The framework further reveals that Bell nonlocality remains shareable in bilateral sequential scenarios. These results establish quantum instruments, rather than POVMs alone, as the fundamental constraints on correlation sharing, providing a unified conceptual framework for quantum resource recycling.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03773"></a>
### [Computation of entanglement for quantum states by a Consensus-Based Optimization method](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03773v1)

**Authors:** Michael Herty, Yijia Tang, Yizhou Zhou  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03773v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces new consensus-based optimization algorithms designed to preserve the geometric structure of quantum states when computing entanglement. By evolving particles directly on the Stiefel manifold or using Hermitian-preserving dynamics, the authors achieve more accurate and stable entanglement measurements than traditional projection methods. The introduction of a multi-species approach further improves efficiency by allowing information exchange between different system dimensions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new class of gradient-free, structure-aware numerical tools for characterizing entanglement in high-dimensional systems, which is critical for analyzing the complexity of many-body states and quantum information resources.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Computing the Entanglement of Formation (EoF) for high-dimensional bipartite mixed quantum states, which is a non-convex, computationally intractable optimization problem subject to complex Stiefel manifold constraints.

**Main result.** The proposed structure-preserving CBO methods (Hermitian and Unitary preserving) outperform standard projection-based methods in accuracy and stability, while the multi-species CBO enhances convergence through cross-dimensional information exchange.

**Method.** A stochastic, gradient-free Consensus-Based Optimization (CBO) framework using particle-based SDEs, specifically adapted with exponential integrators and anisotropic diffusion to maintain the algebraic structure of the optimization manifold.

**Model / system.** Bipartite mixed quantum states (density matrices) such as Werner, Isotropic, and Horodecki states, characterized by their dimension and entanglement properties.

**Key observables.** Entanglement of Formation (EoF), Concurrence, and von Neumann entropy of reduced states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of particles (J), drift rate (lambda), noise intensity (sigma), and the dimension of the optimization space (M).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Hermitian-preserving method assumes the existence of a sufficiently large dimension M to capture the rank of the state; the validity of the method relies on the Laplace principle for Gibbs distribution weighting.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the multi-level particle interaction concept, error analysis of EoF as a function of particle number, trajectories of entanglement over time, and performance comparisons between structure-preserving, projection, and multi-species CBO methods across different quantum states.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the entanglement optimization problem on the Stiefel manifold, proposes two structure-preserving CBO variants and a multi-species extension, provides mathematical proofs for structure preservation and convergence, and validates the methods through numerical experiments on Werner, Isotropic, and Horodecki states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The computation of quantum entanglement can be formulated as a high-dimensional nonconvex optimization problem with orthogonality constraints. In this work, we propose structure-preserving consensus-based optimization (CBO) methods for entanglement computation, with one approach based on a Hermitian formulation and the other evolving directly on the unitary manifold. To handle the variable dimension of the feasible set, we introduce a cross-dimensional interaction mechanism allowing exchange of information between particles of different sizes. Numerical experiments demonstrate that the proposed methods achieve accurate approximations.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a numerical method to map the relationship between quantum discord and Bell non-locality in two-qubit systems. It identifies the minimum amount of discord needed to maintain specific levels of Bell violation across various Bell inequalities. The results reveal how different measurement settings and noise levels influence the bounds of these quantum correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides fundamental insights into the hierarchy of non-classical correlations, which is crucial for understanding the resource requirements for quantum tasks in quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the relationship and correlations between quantum discord and Bell non-locality, specifically seeking the minimum discord required for a bipartite state to exhibit a specific level of Bell violation.

**Main result.** The authors establish lower bounds for discord across six Bell expressions, finding that discord vanishes at the classical limit and that increasing measurement settings generally lowers the required discord. They also suggest the existence of two distinct classes of optimized states based on their minimized discord.

**Method.** A numerical framework using a purification-based approach for state parametrization, combined with global optimization techniques including Basin-hopping and the COBYQA algorithm.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on bipartite two-qubit mixed states, represented via a purification-based scheme with 15 optimization variables including eigenvalues and local rotation angles.

**Key observables.** Quantum discord (D), Bell violation (|B|), and the fraction of the Tsirelson bound (p).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The noise parameter (p), representing the fraction of the maximal quantum Bell value, and the number of measurement settings in different Bell inequalities.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to two-level systems (qubits) and assumes the use of projective measurements for the definition of quantum discord.

**Figures summary.** Figures 1-3 show the lower bounds of discord for various Bell families (CHSH, ModCHSH, Braunstein-Caves, and I-family) as a function of the Bell violation fraction p; Figure 4 provides a summary comparison of all six expressions; Figures 5-7 show detailed numerical optimization landscapes and local minima for specific expressions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of discord-nonlocality correlations, describes the numerical framework and state parametrization, presents the comparative analysis of six Bell inequalities, discusses the optimization landscape and state classification, and concludes with limitations and future directions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a numerical framework for the certification and systematic analysis of the relationship between Bell nonlocality and quantum discord. By determining the minimum discord required for a bipartite state to manifest a specific Bell violation, we establish lower bounds for these correlations. We evaluate this methodology across six distinct Bell expressions, comparing their performance through the minimal discord values observed under varying intensities of white noise. Analysis of the resulting optimization landscape suggests the existence of two characteristic classes of optimized states, categorized by their minimized quantum discord.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03565"></a>
### [Neural optimization for quantum architectures: graph embedding problems with Distance Encoder Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03565v1)

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

To have more chance and get feasible embeddings, the graphs instances are created by randomly setting initial...</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a neural network-based approach to solve the difficult task of embedding graphs onto the physical layout of neutral-atom quantum computers. By using a specialized autoencoder, the method efficiently finds spatial configurations that satisfy strict physical distance constraints. This provides a scalable alternative to classical optimization for preparing quantum hardware for specific computational tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses a critical bottleneck in the NISQ era: the mapping of abstract computational problems onto the physical geometry of Rydberg atom arrays, using machine learning to automate hardware-specific embedding.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of graph embedding, specifically finding a spatial realization of a graph (the Constrained Unit Disk Graph problem) to represent QUBO problems on neutral-atom-based quantum hardware.

**Main result.** The proposed Distance Encoder Network (DEN) outperforms classical solvers like Ipopt in finding feasible embeddings, especially as dimensionality increases, although classical solvers may achieve a better adjacency gap when successful.

**Method.** A neural-enhanced optimization framework using a modified autoencoder (Distance Encoder Network) and a custom Embedding Loss Function (ELF) to learn spatial transformations from non-feasible to feasible solutions.

**Model / system.** The application context is neutral-atom-based quantum hardware using Rydberg atoms in 2D or 3D registers, where the blockade effect determines connectivity via a spin Hamiltonian (Ising model).

**Key observables.** Adjacency gap, feasibility of the embedding, and computational time/scaling.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Minimum atom distance (4 um), maximum register radius (50 um), interaction radius (~10.26 um), and dimensionality (N=2 or 3).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The Fruchterman-Reingold initialization assumes a specific equilibrium distance; the framework assumes the problem can be mapped to a spatial transformation task.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows adjacency matrices and feasible/unfeasible embeddings; Figure 2 illustrates clique and degree-constrained embeddings; Figure 3 depicts the optimization framework; Figure 4 shows scaling of computation time and epoch-to-feasibility; Figure 5 and 6 compare DEN performance against Ipopt in terms of feasibility and adjacency gap.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the CUDG problem, defines the physical hardware constraints, proposes the DEN architecture and ELF loss function, details the experimental setup and dataset generation, and concludes with a comparative performance analysis against classical solvers.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum machines are among the most promising technologies expected to provide significant improvements in the following years. However, bridging the gap between real-world applications and their implementation on quantum hardware is still a complicated task. One of the main challenges is to represent through qubits (i.e., the basic units of quantum information) the problems of interest. According to the specific technology underlying the quantum machine, it is necessary to implement a proper representation strategy, generally referred to as embedding. This paper introduces a neural-enhanced optimization framework to solve the constrained unit disk problem, which arises in the context of qubits positioning for neutral atoms-based quantum hardware. The proposed approach involves a modified autoencoder model, i.e., the Distances Encoder Network, and a custom loss, i.e., the Embedding Loss Function, respectively, to compute Euclidean distances and model the optimization constraints. The core idea behind this design relies on the capability of neural networks to approximate non-linear transformations to make the Distances Encoder Network learn the spatial transformation that maps initial non-feasible solutions of the constrained unit disk problem into feasible ones. The proposed approach outperforms classical solvers, given fixed comparable computation times, and paves the way to address other optimization problems through a similar strategy.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03564"></a>
### [Quantum Vault: Secure Token Authentication Without Classical State Information Benchmarked on IBMQ](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03564v1)

**Authors:** Lucas Tsunaki, Boris Naydenov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03564v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Attack scenarios on previous quantum token proposals with classical side information. In general, a token protocol has three steps: I. a bank issues quantum tokens in states |ψ⟩with angles (θ, ϕ), II. the bank transmits the tokens to the user who stores them until authentication, III. a vendor or bank authenticates the tokens by measuring their states and comparing with the classically stored values of (θ, ϕ). While the token stored by the user is no-cloning protected, an attacker could steal the values of (θ, ϕ) from the bank to create fake tokens, with the bank not even realizing it was hacked.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

In the quantum vault protocol the bank issues two Haar random identical tokens in the state |ψ1⟩= |ψ2⟩, one of which is stored in the bank’s vault while the other is given to the user. This, way both parties are no-cloning protected from hacker attacks. For authentication, the bank or vendor performs a SWAP test repeated N times in order to verify if both tokens are in the same state or if one of them is fake.</figcaption>
</figure>

As Θ increases and the states become orthogonal, ¯C20 increases, enabling the bank or vendor to verify if the two states |ψ1⟩and |ψ2⟩are the same. (c) Distribution of C20(Θ = 0) taking 400 angles (θ1, ϕ1) = (θ2, ϕ2) uniformly distributed in the Bloch sphere. From this, the acceptance threshold τ is defined such that the probability that the bank...</figcaption>

The distribution from fake tokens is shifted to higher values of C20, but still the area below τ corresponding to the probability to accept a fake token pf is considerable. **Summary.** This paper introduces a 'Quantum Vault' protocol for token authentication that removes the need to store classical state information, preventing hackers from forging tokens via stolen database data. By storing a physical quantum copy at the bank, the protocol achieves extremely low forgery rates on current noisy quantum hardware. The approach provides a scalable path toward secure, traceable, and revocable quantum authentication.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing quantum token protocols are vulnerable to attacks where hackers steal classical side-information (like Bloch sphere angles) from the issuing agent to forge tokens. The goal is to create a protocol that eliminates the need for storing classical state information.

**Main result.** The proposed 'Quantum Vault' protocol achieves high security, with forgery probabilities as low as 10^-18 for a 200-token bill, even on noisy IBMQ hardware. It demonstrates that removing classical data protects against bank-side database compromises.

**Method.** The authors use a SWAP test to compare a user's token against a physical quantum copy stored in a bank's vault. They benchmark the protocol using the Qiskit SDK on three IBM Quantum (IBMQ) processors (Kingston, Fez, and Marrakesh).

**Model / system.** The system consists of two identical Haar random qubit states prepared via rotation operators. The experimental platform uses IBMQ Heron r2 family superconducting processors.

**Key observables.** The SWAP test observable (average of the bitstring C_N), quality parameters (oscillation amplitude Q_a and offset Q_o), and the acceptance threshold (tau).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of tokens in a bill (M), number of SWAP test repetitions (N), and the target probability of accepting genuine tokens (p_b = 0.99).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the existence of a quantum channel between the bank and the agent, and assumes the use of error mitigation/correction to prevent decoherence during storage.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates classical side-information attack scenarios; Figure 2 shows the Quantum Vault scheme; Figure 4 shows the density distribution of the SWAP test observable and the exponential decay of forgery probability with increasing token numbers; Figure 5 displays hardware-specific measurement outcomes.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies a vulnerability in current quantum tokens, proposes the Quantum Vault architecture, describes the hardware-agnostic implementation, benchmarks the protocol on IBMQ hardware, and performs a security analysis against query attacks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum tokens are underlying primitives for quantum money and network proposals, which leverage the no-cloning theorem to realize unforgeable authentication. A relevant but overlooked type of attack to such architectures is a hacker that steals the classical side information of the token states from the issuing agent (e.g. a bank), allowing the forgery of fake tokens without violating no-cloning theorem. Our proposal avoids this threat by removing classical side information about the token states, where instead a copy of the token is stored at the bank, i.e. a quantum vault. This copy can be accessed by anyone to perform authentication, consuming the token pair in the process. Our protocol is benchmarked and quality parameters are identified within a hardware agnostic framework employing three cloud-based IBM quantum (IBMQ) processors, such that the protocol is applicable to arbitrary quantum platforms. By comparing the efficiency with which genuine tokens are produced and authenticated with a possible query attack scenario, we demonstrate the security of the protocol. Where we achieve probabilities lower than $10^{-4}$ for false-negative errors and $10^{-18}$ for successful attacks when considering quantum bills composed of 200 tokens, even in the worst performing hardware. The quantum vault not only symmetrically protects both user and bank with the same quantum principles, but provides a step towards public key authentication, since any untrusted party can have authentication access granted from the bank to the tokens without being able to clone them, assuming they have a quantum channel with the vault. Besides public accessible verifiability, our proposal naturally achieves standard unforgeability, traceability and revocability.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper extends the concept of the Dissipative Quantum Limit from stationary to non-stationary quantum sensors. It demonstrates that while the DQL does not limit the sensitivity of a single known signal in non-stationary systems, it imposes a new uncertainty relation when measuring multiple signal quadratures simultaneously. This provides a deeper understanding of the fundamental limits of precision in time-varying quantum measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems as it redefines the fundamental precision limits for time-dependent sensing protocols and explores the boundaries of the Standard Quantum Limit.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether the Dissipative Quantum Limit (DQL), which constrains the sensitivity of stationary quantum sensors, also applies to non-stationary quantum sensors.

**Main result.** The author finds that for a single known signal shape, the DQL does not inherently limit sensitivity in non-stationary systems, but a DQL-like uncertainty relation emerges when attempting to simultaneously measure multiple signal quadratures.

**Method.** The study employs linear measurement theory, commutator-based analysis, and the quantum Cramér-Rao bound to optimize noise spectral density under uncertainty constraints.

**Model / system.** The model consists of a linear quantum force sensor comprising a probe subsystem and a meter subsystem, such as optomechanical sensors or gravitational-wave detectors, subject to signal, thermal, and back-action forces.

**Key observables.** Sum noise spectral density, signal-to-noise ratio (SNR), and the quadrature amplitudes of the signal force.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Susceptibility function (chi), dynamic rigidity (K), dissipation (friction coefficient H), and the Standard Quantum Limit (SQL) and Dissipative Quantum Limit (DQL).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The sensors are assumed to be linear; the dissipation in the probe is assumed to be stationary; the meter is analyzed in a memoryless case where dynamic rigidity is zero.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of a generic linear force sensor illustrating the interaction between the probe, meter, signal force, thermal force, and back-action force.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by extending the DQL analysis from stationary to non-stationary systems, moves to a mathematical framework using linear measurement theory, analyzes a specific memoryless meter case study, and concludes with the derivation of uncertainty relations for multiple signal quadratures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The concept of the dissipative quantum limit (DQL) was first put forward in 1980s and was analyzed in detail much later in Ref. [Phys. Rev. A 103, 043721 (2021)] for the particular case of stationary (invariant with respect to a shift of time) systems. Here we extend that analysis to the general non-stationary case.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03794"></a>
### [Caustics and catastrophes in strong-field physics -- Picard--Lefschetz theory as a universal approach to saddle-point methods in attosecond science](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03794v1)

**Authors:** Anne Weber  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03794v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This thesis introduces Picard-Lefschetz theory as a universal approach to solving the oscillatory integrals found in attosecond science. By providing a way to handle caustics where standard saddle-point methods fail, it enables a more systematic analysis of high-order harmonic generation and strong-field ionization. This advancement improves the theoretical understanding and potential control of ultrafast light-matter interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a robust mathematical tool for handling singularities and caustics in light-matter interactions, which is crucial for the precise control of attosecond pulse generation and understanding non-linear quantum dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a general mathematical framework for applying saddle-point methods to highly oscillatory integrals in strong-field physics, particularly when trajectories coalesce at caustics.

**Main result.** The development of practical numerical methods using Picard-Lefschetz theory to evaluate oscillatory integrals and identify individual critical point contributions, even in caustic regions where standard approximations fail.

**Method.** Application of Picard-Lefschetz theory to decompose highly oscillatory integrals into contributions from distinct saddle points of the semi-classical action.

**Model / system.** The system involves atoms interacting with intense, ultrashort laser pulses, specifically within the context of high-order harmonic generation (HHG) and strong-field ionization.

**Key observables.** High-order harmonic spectrum and electron trajectories.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser field parameters, driving laser configuration, and the attosecond timescale.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The physics is described using a 'quantum orbits' model based on semi-classical action and interfering electron trajectories.

**Paper structure.** The work introduces Picard-Lefschetz theory, develops numerical implementation methods, and then applies these techniques to the study of caustics in strong-field ionization and HHG.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ultrashort laser pulses on the attosecond timescale are typically achieved via high-order harmonic generation (HHG), a nonlinear process in which atoms interact with intense light fields to emit a broad spectrum of harmonics. HHG is commonly described in terms of a `quantum orbits' model based on several interfering electron trajectories, thereby incorporating both quantum-mechanical effects and an intuitive picture of classical dynamics. By tuning the parameters of the driving laser field, the interplay between these trajectories can be controlled, shaping the emitted light. Mathematically, this model expresses the harmonic response as a highly oscillatory integral. Applying saddle-point methods to this integral allows it to be decomposed into contributions from distinct saddle points of the semi-classical action, thereby linking quantum dynamics to classical trajectories. However, a general framework for applying these methods across arbitrary parameters and laser configurations has been missing.   In this thesis, we introduce Picard--Lefschetz theory and develop practical numerical methods for its application. These enable the evaluation of oscillatory integrals and identification of contributions from individual critical points.   We apply these techniques to strong-field ionisation and HHG, focusing on caustics -- enhancement features where trajectories coalesce and standard approximations fail. Our methods remain valid in these regions, allowing systematic analysis of parameter regimes and revealing previously inaccessible features. This work improves the understanding and control of ultrafast light--matter interactions.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03995"></a>
### [Quantum Dispersive Waves and Multimode Squeezing in Pure-Kerr Parametrically Driven Cavity Solitons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03995v1)

**Authors:** Rafael Romero Mendez, Sashank Kaushik Sridhar, Samyak Gothi, Pradyoth Shandilya, Yichen Shen, Curtis Menyuk, Avik Dutt  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03995v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper provides the first multimode quantum analysis of pure-Kerr parametrically driven cavity solitons. It demonstrates that these solitons can generate significant squeezing (up to 20 dB) and exhibit quantum-analogous dispersive waves. These findings offer a new way to achieve strong multimode quantum noise reduction for metrology and nonlinear dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It is highly relevant for quantum optics and open quantum systems as it identifies a pathway to achieve high-level (20 dB) multimode squeezing and explores the quantum-classical correspondence in nonlinear wave phenomena.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to provide the first multimode quantum description of pure-Kerr parametrically driven cavity solitons (PDCS) and explore their quantum noise properties.

**Main result.** The authors uncover single- and two-mode squeezing in the below-threshold regime and identify novel 'quantum' dispersive waves (the quantum analog of soliton Cherenkov radiation) above threshold, demonstrating up to 20 dB of squeezing.

**Method.** The study employs a multimode quantum description of the nonlinear dynamics within the cavity.

**Model / system.** The system consists of pure-Kerr parametrically driven cavity solitons (PDCS), which are localized optical pulses arising from parametric processes in a Kerr medium.

**Key observables.** Single-mode squeezing, two-mode squeezing, and quantum dispersive waves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Below threshold and above threshold regimes; squeezing levels limited by overcoupling and intrinsic losses.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a pure-Kerr medium and considers losses primarily through overcoupling and intrinsic mechanisms.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces PDCS, presents a multimode quantum description, analyzes squeezing in the below-threshold regime, investigates quantum dispersive waves above threshold, and discusses experimental feasibility regarding squeezing levels.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Parametrically driven cavity solitons (PDCS), unlike single-pumped cavity solitons, are localized optical pulses arising from parametric processes. These cavity solitons, recently discovered in pure-Kerr media, offer great promise for nonlinear dynamics studies and metrology. Here, we present the first multimode quantum description of pure-Kerr PDCS. In the below threshold regime, we verify single- and two-mode squeezing, while above threshold we uncover novel "quantum" dispersive waves - the quantum analog of soliton Cherenkov radiation. Besides revealing these unexplored quantum properties, we show that PDCS generates up to 20 dB of squeezing, only limited by overcoupling and intrinsic losses for experimentally routine parameters. We therefore provide a pathway to observe strong multimode quantum noise reduction in these systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03682"></a>
### [Resource-efficient parallel entanglement generation for multinode quantum networks via time-bin multiplexing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03682v1)

**Authors:** Wenbo Zhang, Jing Zheng, Yimin Wang, Tao Li  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03682v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03682_figures/2605.03682_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic of the compact protocol for generating two three- qubit GHZ states in parallel. Each node has two cavity-qubit units, each controlled by an optical switch to control the photon-qubit inter- action. The photonic time-bin qudit interacts probabilistically with the qubits in node A, and then travels to nodes B and C, where it in- teracts with the qubits in the same manner. At the final node, the pho- ton is measured by an X-basis detector, and upon successful detec- tion, the measurement outcome is communicated to the other nodes, thereby enabling the generation of two three-qubit GHZ states across three nodes.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a resource-efficient protocol for generating multiple GHZ states in parallel across a quantum network using time-bin multiplexing. By using a single photon to entangle multiple nodes simultaneously, the method reduces the complexity of optical modulations and the necessary coherence time for stationary qubits. This approach offers a scalable pathway for building large-scale multinode quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a scalable architecture for quantum networks that drastically relaxes the stringent requirements on qubit coherence and experimental control, which is highly relevant for developing practical quantum repeaters and distributed quantum computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Generating multipartite entanglement across multiple remote quantum nodes is limited by exponential photon loss in optical fibers and requires high coherence times and complex control.

**Main result.** The proposed protocol enables parallel generation of M GHZ states among N nodes using time-bin multiplexing, significantly reducing the required coherence time for stationary qubits and the complexity of photonic modulations.

**Method.** A time-bin qudit-encoding protocol where a single photon interacts with cavity-qubit units via optical switches, inducing controlled-phase flips, followed by measurement in a generalized X-basis.

**Model / system.** A network of N spatially separated nodes, each containing M stationary qubits coupled to single-sided cavities, modeled using a four-level atom system and cavity-QED dynamics.

**Key observables.** Average fidelity (F_M,N) and average efficiency (eta_M,N) of the generated GHZ states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Node distance (L0), photon qudit dimension (d=2M), cooperativity (Cs), detuning (Delta_s), and qubit coherence times (T1, T2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dimension of the photonic qudit is strictly tied to the number of parallel GHZ states being generated (d=2M).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the protocol for M=2 and N=3, illustrating the photon's path through nodes A, B, and C and its interaction with cavity-qubit units.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of scaling quantum networks, proposes a time-bin multiplexing protocol, describes the physical model and interaction mechanism, presents numerical results for fidelity and efficiency, and compares the protocol's advantages against conventional schemes.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonlocal entanglement generation among multiple remote quantum nodes provides a critical foundation for a variety of counterintuitive quantum applications. The exponential loss of photons transmitting over optical fibers sets an upper limit for entangling these quantum nodes. Here, we propose a resource-efficient and parallel protocol for entangling multiple remote quantum nodes via time-bin multiplexing. The transmission of a single photon with qudit-encoding in the time-bin mode enables entangling multiple stationary qubits in parallel, when single photons and individual stationary qubits interfaces are used and photon-state modulations are properly introduced before subsequently impinging the photon into each interface. Our protocol can generate parallel multipartite entanglement among ($N\geq3$) quantum nodes with the dimension of the photonic time bins independent of $N$, exponentially reducing the requirements for the coherence time of the stationary qubits and for the complexity of the photonic modulations. These distinct features make our protocol particularly advantageous for the development of multinode quantum networks.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03807"></a>
### [The Geometric Part of Decoherence: Quasi-Orthogonality in High-Dimensional Hilbert Spaces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03807v1)

**Authors:** Karl Svozil  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03807v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03807_figures/2605.03807_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03807_figures/2605.03807_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03807_figures/2605.03807_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03807_figures/2605.03807_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03807_figures/2605.03807_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper identifies a geometric mechanism for decoherence based on the fact that in high-dimensional Hilbert spaces, almost all states are nearly orthogonal. This 'kinematic' property provides an enormous capacity for environmental records, ensuring that macroscopic quantum superpositions are suppressed. The effect is most robust in chaotic systems where the accessible Hilbert space dimension is exponentially large.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental geometric perspective on the emergence of classicality and the effectiveness of decoherence, which is highly relevant for researchers studying open quantum systems and the transition from quantum to classical regimes.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the geometric/kinematic contribution to decoherence, specifically addressing why macroscopic quantum alternatives do not blur into a featureless state in high-dimensional Hilbert spaces.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that high-dimensional Hilbert spaces possess an exponentially large capacity for mutually quasi-orthogonal environmental records, providing a geometric mechanism that suppresses macroscopic interference.

**Method.** The study employs high-dimensional geometry, including the concentration of measure on spheres, Lévy's Lemma, and the Beta distribution to analyze the overlap of Haar-random vectors.

**Model / system.** A system coupled to a macroscopic environment modeled as a high-dimensional Hilbert space, where the effective dimension scales with the microcanonical entropy of the environment.

**Key observables.** The squared overlap between environmental branch states, |<Ei(t)|Ej(t)>|^2, and the survival probability of significant overlaps.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension (d) of the Hilbert space, the epsilon-quasi-orthogonality threshold, and the effective dimension (deff) related to microcanonical entropy.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The argument is conditional on the dynamics being able to populate the available geometric reservoir; it assumes the Hamiltonian drives the system into typical regions of the accessible subspace.

**Paper structure.** The paper first establishes the geometric mechanism of quasi-orthogonality, then distinguishes between dynamical and kinematic ingredients of decoherence, and finally examines how dynamical constraints (like integrability or MBL) affect the effective dimension and the validity of the geometric reservoir.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We isolate a geometric mechanism that complements the dynamical suppression of macroscopic interference: In a high-dimensional Hilbert space, almost all state vectors are nearly orthogonal, accommodating an exponentially large reservoir of mutually quasi-orthogonal environmental records. This geometry explains why macroscopic alternatives fail to exhibit visible interference once such records are populated. The argument is conditional and finite-dimensional, and it leaves the interpretive core of quantum mechanics untouched: geometry alone does not select a pointer basis, does not guarantee that a given Hamiltonian drives the system into typical regions of the accessible subspace, and does not turn an improper mixture into a proper one. It merely supplies the vast Hilbert-space capacity that makes decoherence so overwhelmingly effective for all practical purposes.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03629"></a>
### [Adversarial Effects on Expressibility and Trainability in Distributed Variational Quantum Algorithms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03629v1)

**Authors:** Abhishek Sadhu, Sharu Theresa Jose  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03629v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

(b) Kraus expressiblity and solution coverage: Let SA and SB denote solution sets within the unitary group for problems A and B. Adversarially-induced noisy non-local gates restrict the accessible transformation space to T . Kraus expressibility measures how uniformly T explores the unitary group: low expressibility limits coverage (e.g., only SA), whereas high expressibility enables broader coverage but leads to barren plateaus.</figcaption>

(a) For fixed trainable parameters, Kraus expressibility norm decays with PQCh-ansatz depth and adversarial noise. (b) Adversarial noise in non-local CNOTs (via reduced concurrence) renders even shallow circuits untrainable. (c) At fixed depth, stronger adversarial noise (low concurrence) reduces gradient variance thereby impeding optimization. The plots are averaged over 100 trials.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper explores how an adversary can corrupt the shared entanglement used in distributed quantum computing to sabotage variational algorithms. It introduces a new metric called Kraus expressibility to quantify how noise affects the ability of a circuit to represent solutions and shows that attackers can bias results without triggering the vanishing gradients known as barren plateaus.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum networks as it provides a rigorous framework for understanding how structured noise in quantum communication channels directly degrades the performance and security of distributed quantum algorithms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how adversarial perturbations to shared entanglement in distributed quantum computing impact the expressibility and trainability of Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs).

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that an adversary can manipulate entanglement-level noise to bias optimization toward incorrect solutions while avoiding detection via barren plateaus, and they establish a new metric, Kraus expressibility, to quantify this effect.

**Method.** The study uses a Kraus representation framework to map entanglement perturbations to gate-level noise, introduces the Kraus expressibility norm, and employs gradient variance analysis to link noise to trainability.

**Model / system.** A distributed quantum architecture consisting of multiple NISQ processors connected via a quantum repeater network using the cat-entangler/cat-disentangler protocol for non-local CNOT gates.

**Key observables.** Kraus expressibility norm, cost function gradient variance, concurrence, and state purity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (L), number of qubits (n), degree of parameter initialization confinement (r), and entanglement concurrence (C).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on adversarial attacks via the entanglement-sharing layer and assumes the use of the cat-entangler protocol for non-local gates.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates different adversarial models (network vs. device level) and the restriction of solution space; Figure 2 shows energy convergence failure in VQE under adversarial noise; Figure 3 shows the decay of Kraus expressibility and gradient variance with depth and noise; Figure 4 shows the scaling of gradient variance with qubit number under different noise levels.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the vulnerability of distributed VQAs, introduces a mathematical framework for mapping entanglement noise to Kraus operators, defines the new Kraus expressibility metric, provides analytical bounds on gradient variance, and validates the theory through numerical simulations of VQE and scaling analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Distributed quantum algorithms offer a promising pathway to scale variational quantum algorithms beyond the constraints of noisy intermediate-scale quantum hardware. However, existing approaches implicitly assume a trusted entanglement-sharing layer across quantum processors. We show that this assumption introduces a fundamental vulnerability: adversarial perturbations of shared entanglement induce structured gate-level noise that directly impacts quantum learning. We develop a framework that maps entanglement-level perturbations to gate-level noise via an explicit Kraus representation. To quantify their impact, we introduce Kraus expressibility, a metric that generalizes unitary expressibility to noisy quantum channels. We then establish a trade-off between Kraus expressibility and trainability of noisy quantum circuits through gradient variance analysis. Our analysis reveals that an adversary can manipulate Kraus expressibility to maintain sufficiently large cost gradients (avoiding barren plateaus) while systematically biasing optimization toward incorrect solutions. We validate these findings through numerical simulations, demonstrating adversarial degradation of expressibility and trainability.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors present a Graph Neural Network that operates directly on gauge-invariant Wilson loops to simulate Abelian lattice gauge theories. The model efficiently predicts both static properties and semiclassical dynamics, offering a powerful tool for studying complex gauge-matter interactions without the overhead of redundant gauge degrees of freedom.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a scalable way to simulate the time evolution of gauge-matter systems, bypassing the computational bottleneck of repeated exact diagonalization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient machine learning framework for Abelian lattice gauge theories that handles gauge redundancy and captures nonlocal correlations induced by gauge-matter coupling.

**Main result.** The proposed gauge-invariant GNN accurately predicts local and global observables in Z2 and U(1) models and serves as a scalable surrogate for semiclassical dynamics, capturing statistical properties like autocorrelation functions.

**Method.** A Graph Neural Network (GNN) architecture using gauge-invariant Wilson loop inputs and message passing, combined with automatic differentiation to reconstruct gauge-covariant forces for dynamics.

**Model / system.** Abelian lattice gauge theories (Z2 and U(1)) and U(1) quantum link models on a square lattice, featuring gauge-matter coupling via a tight-binding Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Wilson loops (plaquette and winding), local fermion density, total energy, and autocorrelation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Inverse temperature (beta), chemical potential (mu), kinetic scale (tau), electric coupling (g), and magnetic strength (K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The architecture is specifically tailored for Abelian theories and assumes the gauge-invariant sector can be captured by local and global Wilson loops; dynamical results are currently limited to the semiclassical regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the GNN architecture, showing the mapping from gauge configurations to Wilson loops and the message-passing process. Figure 2 contains heat maps comparing GNN predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a gauge-invariant GNN architecture, benchmarks its performance on static observables in Z2 and U(1) models, and then demonstrates its utility as a surrogate for semiclassical time evolution in quantum link models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Local gauge structures play a central role in a wide range of condensed matter systems and synthetic quantum platforms, where they emerge as effective descriptions of strongly correlated phases and engineered dynamics. We introduce a gauge-invariant graph neural network (GNN) architecture for Abelian lattice gauge models, in which symmetry is enforced explicitly through local gauge-invariant inputs, such as Wilson loops, and preserved throughout message passing, eliminating redundant gauge degrees of freedom while retaining expressive power. We benchmark the approach on both $\mathbb{Z}_2$ and $\mathrm{U}(1)$ lattice gauge models, achieving accurate predictions of global observables and spatially resolved quantities despite the nonlocal correlations induced by gauge-matter coupling. We further demonstrate that the learned model serves as an efficient surrogate for semiclassical dynamics in $\mathrm{U}(1)$ quantum link models, enabling stable and scalable time evolution without repeated fermionic diagonalization, while faithfully reproducing both local dynamics and statistical correlations. These results establish gauge-invariant message passing as a compact and physically grounded framework for learning and simulating Abelian lattice gauge systems.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03978"></a>
### [Phase-Reference Control of Steady-State Entanglement in Open Quantum Systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03978v1)

**Authors:** Areeda Ayoub, Alfonso Castillo-Gonzalez, Eric R Bittner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03978v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper shows that the phase of a squeezed reservoir can be used to control steady-state entanglement in open quantum systems. It demonstrates that local dissipation can generate entanglement when paired with coherent coupling, and that the effectiveness of this process depends on the phase reference of the reservoir. This establishes phase-sensitive reservoir engineering as a viable route for creating entanglement in continuous-variable quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new mechanism for reservoir engineering, showing that the phase of the environment is a controllable knob for generating nonlocal correlations in continuous-variable systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the phase reference of a phase-sensitive reservoir can be used to control and generate steady-state entanglement in open quantum systems.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that local, phase-sensitive dissipation can generate entanglement when combined with coherent coupling, and that the resulting entanglement structure depends explicitly on the reservoir's phase reference.

**Method.** A covariance-matrix approach for Gaussian-preserving dynamics is used to analyze the steady state of the system.

**Model / system.** Continuous-variable open quantum systems subject to phase-sensitive (squeezed) dissipation and coherent coupling.

**Key observables.** Steady-state entanglement (quantified via entanglement measures for Gaussian states) and thermal robustness of entanglement.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing strength, coherent coupling strength, and the phase reference of the reservoir (comparing phase-locked/rotating-frame vs. laboratory-frame implementations).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are assumed to be Gaussian-preserving, allowing for the use of the covariance-matrix formalism.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of phase-reference control, applies a covariance-matrix approach to Gaussian dynamics, analyzes the role of squeezing and coupling, compares different phase-reference frames, and concludes with the implications for reservoir engineering.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that steady-state entanglement in open quantum systems is controlled by the phase reference of a phase-sensitive reservoir. Using a covariance-matrix approach for Gaussian-preserving dynamics, we demonstrate that purely local, phase-sensitive dissipation can generate entanglement when combined with coherent coupling. The steady state exhibits a finite entangled region with an optimal squeezing strength that maximizes both the magnitude and thermal robustness of entanglement. We find that coherent coupling does not enhance entanglement monotonically, but instead regulates the conversion of local squeezing into nonlocal correlations. Importantly, the coupling dependence is controlled by the phase reference of the squeezed reservoir: phase-locked (rotating-frame) and laboratory-frame implementations yield qualitatively distinct steady states and entanglement structure. These results establish phase-sensitive reservoir engineering as a controllable route to steady-state entanglement in continuous-variable systems. Steady-state entanglement in phase-sensitive open systems depends explicitly on the reservoir phase reference and is not invariant under changes of that reference.}

</details>

**Summary.** This paper is a formal rebuttal defending the use of quantum information metrics to study spin correlations in particle physics. The authors argue that the complex dynamics of QCD fragmentation can be validly modeled as an internal environment affecting the entanglement and steering of produced baryon pairs.

**Why it may be interesting.** It explores the application of open quantum system concepts (decoherence and non-Markovianity) to a high-energy particle production process, treating particle interactions as an internal environment.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The authors are rebutting a critique regarding the physical validity of using quantum information concepts, such as decoherence and quantum steering, to describe particle production in high-energy physics.

**Main result.** The authors defend their model by demonstrating that QCD fragmentation and hadronization can be treated as an effective internal environment that influences spin correlations.

**Method.** The authors use quantum information theory tools, including quantum steering, entanglement, and discord, applied to reconstructed spin density matrices from angular distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper is structured as a point-by-point rebuttal, addressing the physical basis of the environment, the validity of steering measurements, and the rejection of operational measurement constraints.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We thank the Commentator for his detailed critique, which provides an opportunity to clarify the physical foundations of our work. While we appreciate the emphasis on rigor when applying quantum information concepts to high-energy physics (HEP), we respectfully disagree with the assertion that our assumptions lack a physical or operational basis. In the following sections, we address each point raised, demonstrating that our modeling is grounded in the established physics of QCD fragmentation and production dynamics in $e^+e^-\to Λ\barΛ$, as supported by recent experimental and theoretical advancements. Our approach treats the system as an effective open bipartite system during the intrinsic production stage, rather than during post-production propagation, which aligns with the analyses cited in our references.

(iv) An adjoint simulation is performed, yielding the adjoint fields. (v) Combining the adjoint and eigenmode fields, a geometric gradient is obtained.
