**Summary.** This paper presents Arqade, an automated framework for designing efficient predecoders for any qLDPC code to alleviate the classical computational burden of quantum error correction. By handling simple errors in a lightweight first layer, it significantly reduces the workload on complex second-level decoders, enabling much more scalable quantum-classical interfaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The scaling of fault-tolerant quantum computing is bottlenecked by the high classical computational cost and resource contention at the quantum-classical interface, particularly for complex qLDPC codes.

**Main result.** The Arqade framework automates the generation of lightweight predecoders that can reduce decoder utilization by up to 3,963x and OSD utilization by up to 72.71% while maintaining logical error rates.

**Method.** The authors introduce Arqade, an automated framework that uses syndrome measurement circuits and detector error models to generate predecoding primitives, which are then mapped to hardware using a graph coloring approach for pipeline construction.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on quantum error correction using qLDPC codes, including surface, color, bivariate bicycle (BB), and RQT codes, under a circuit-level SI1000 noise model.

**Key observables.** Logical error rate (LER), decoder utilization, decoding latency, predecoding coverage, and hardware metrics like power, area, and LUT utilization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical error rates ($10^{-4}$ to $5 \cdot 10^{-3}$), stabilizer weights, and power limits (e.g., 1.5 W at 4 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The evaluated codes are primarily CSS codes, and the framework assumes the availability of a second-level decoder like BP-OSD.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the decoder allocation problem, the Arqade framework workflow, predecoding primitive examples, pruning strategies, hardware pipeline construction via graph coloring, and performance comparisons of LER and coverage across different code types.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the resource contention problem, defines the Arqade framework for automated predecoder construction, details the hardware pipeline synthesis using graph coloring, presents performance evaluations on various qLDPC codes, and analyzes hardware PPA (Power, Performance, Area) for FPGA and cryogenic ASIC implementations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum-classical interfaces (QCIs) for fault-tolerant quantum computing must manage simultaneous, real-time decoding across thousands to millions of logical qubits. Scaling these architectures necessitates sharing expensive decoding resources among logical qubits, which introduces severe resource contention within the QCI. While resolving these bottlenecks through efficient resource distribution remains a persistent challenge, lightweight predecoding holds promise to alleviate strain on shared decoding components by decreasing average latency and decoder usage. Notably, research into both decoder allocation and predecoding has been strictly confined to the surface code. With the growing emphasis on general quantum low-density parity-check (qLDPC) codes, slower decoding speeds will intensify resource contention, while the inherent complexity of these codes will render manual predecoder design unfeasible.   To address this gap, we introduce an automated framework designed to generate predecoders for arbitrary qLDPC codes. These automatically constructed predecoders autonomously process over 90% of the decoding workload, cutting overall decoder utilization by up to 3,963x. This includes a reduction of up to 72.71% in computationally demanding ordered statistics decoding (OSD). Furthermore, we detail a highly efficient, pipelined hardware design that allows for the concurrent decoding of approximately 1,200 bivariate bicycle (BB) code logical qubits using a single FPGA. When implemented as a cryogenic ASIC, the architecture scales to support between 36,000 and 360,000 BB code logical qubits, operating within a 1.5 W power limit at 4 K.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper uses neutron scattering to characterize the magnetic ground state of the bilayer nickelate La3Ni2O7. It reveals a stripe-type magnetic order with significant interlayer coupling and a unique spectrum of spin excitations. These findings provide a new magnetic framework that differs from cuprates and is critical for understanding superconductivity in nickelates.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to resolve the nature of magnetism and spin dynamics in the ambient-pressure parent phase of bilayer nickelates (La3Ni2O7), which is essential for understanding the pairing mechanism in their high-temperature superconducting phase.

**Main result.** The researchers identified a single-stripe magnetic order with strong interlayer antiferromagnetic coupling and anisotropic in-plane dispersions, establishing a magnetic framework that is distinct from cuprates due to enhanced mid-energy spin excitations.

**Method.** The study uses inelastic and elastic neutron scattering on high-quality single crystals grown via the optical floating-zone method, with data analysis involving linear spin-wave theory simulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the bilayer nickelate La3Ni2O7, modeled using a bilayer Heisenberg Hamiltonian that includes interlayer exchange (Jc), next-nearest-neighbor coupling (J2), and anisotropic in-plane couplings (J1a, J1b) within a stripe-type magnetic order.

**Key observables.** Magnetic Bragg peaks, spin-excitation dispersions, spin gap, L-dependent intensity modulations, and local dynamic susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic transition temperature TN ~ 151 K, spin gap ~ 5 meV, spin-wave bandwidth ~ 70 meV, and exchange parameters such as Jc = 39.86 meV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the spin dynamics can be accurately captured by a Heisenberg-type Hamiltonian and relies on the quality of the single-crystal growth to rule out phase separation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the magnetic structure, susceptibility, and Bragg peaks; Figure 2 presents constant-energy slices of excitations; Figure 3 displays measured and calculated spin-wave dispersions; Figure 4 shows constant-energy cuts and the energy dependence of local susceptibility.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation regarding nickelate superconductivity, describes the crystal growth and neutron scattering techniques, presents the magnetic structure and spin dynamics results, compares the findings to cuprates, and concludes with the implications for the superconducting mechanism.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The recent discovery of high-temperature superconductivity in pressurized and thin film nickelates has generated intense interest, yet the nature of magnetism in their ambient-pressure parent phases remains poorly understood, despite its potentially crucial role in pairing. Here we use neutron scattering to resolve the spin order and dynamics of single-crystalline La3Ni2O7, an ambient-pressure parent of this class. Well defined spin excitations are observed at Q = (0, 0.5, 2.5), featuring a~5 meV spin gap and anisotropic in-plane dispersions, with zone-boundary softening along the transverse direction indicative of competing exchange interactions. The excitations exhibit pronounced out-of-plane modulations with bilayer periodicity, providing direct evidence for antiferromagnetic interlayer coupling. Their dispersion is well described by a bilayer Heisenberg Hamiltonian with strong interlayer exchange and competing in-plane couplings within a stripe-type magnetic order. Normalization of the spectra to absolute units reveals that, although the spin-wave bandwidth is only about 25% of that in cuprates, the local dynamic susceptibility at comparable energies is significantly enhanced, yielding a total fluctuating moment of comparable magnitude. These results highlight intense mid-energy spin excitations rooted in substantial electronic correlations as a defining feature of this family, establishing a magnetic framework distinct from cuprates and directly relevant to understanding superconductivity in this system.

</details>

**Summary.** The authors developed a highly efficient quantum Monte Carlo algorithm to study the SO(5) WZW model. They successfully mapped out a phase diagram that includes a multicritical point and provided evidence that the SO(5)-symmetric disordered phase is likely a weakly gapped state, resolving a long-standing debate in the field.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the global phase diagram and the nature of the critical point in the (2+1)D SO(5) nonlinear sigma model with a Wess-Zumino-Witten term.

**Main result.** The study identifies a critical point separating an SO(5)-broken ordered phase from an SO(5)-symmetric disordered phase and demonstrates that the disordered phase is likely a weakly gapped phase rather than a unitary CFT.

**Method.** An optimized continuous-field quantum Monte Carlo (CF-DQMC) algorithm using Langevin dynamics and a Metropolis acceptance condition, which achieves a significant computational speedup.

**Model / system.** A (2+1)D SO(5) nonlinear sigma model with a level-1 Wess-Zumino-Witten term, regularized by projecting onto the lowest Landau level of half-filled Dirac fermions in both spherical and torus geometries.

**Key observables.** SO(5) order parameter, scaling dimensions (delta_v and delta_s), correlation ratios, and the dynamical critical exponent (z).

**Important parameters / regimes.** SO(5) coupling strength (u), anisotropy parameter (alpha), and system size (number of magnetic fluxes, N_phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory is regularized via projection onto the lowest Landau level; the Trotter decomposition introduces a systematic error of O(delta_tau^2).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows RG flow scenarios; Figure 2 displays the SO(5) order parameter and scaling dimensions; Figure 3 illustrates the VBS-disorder transition; Figure 5 provides a benchmark of the new algorithm against DMRG.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical model and the new CF-DQMC algorithm, presents the phase diagram identification on different geometries, discusses the nature of the critical point and the disordered phase, and concludes with the resolution of long-standing RG flow scenarios.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We develop an optimized continuous-field quantum Monte Carlo (QMC) algorithm to investigate the SO(5) nonlinear sigma model with a Wess-Zumino-Witten term, which describes half-filled Dirac fermions in 2+1 space-time dimensions akin to graphene and Yukawa coupled to a quintuplet of compatible mass terms. To regularize the theory, we project onto the lowest Landau level for both spherical and torus geometries. Our algorithm reduces the computational complexity to $O(βN_{\mathbf{q}} N_φ^2)$, yielding a speedup of a factor of $N_φ$ (the number of magnetic fluxes, i.e., system size) relative to prior works [1-3]. This advance enables us to simulate system sizes up to $N_φ=140$ on torus and $N_φ=49$ on sphere, far exceeding the maximum sizes accessed, and to map out the universal phase diagram of the model on both geometries. Most notably, we identify and characterize a critical point that separates an SO(5)-broken ordered phase at small coupling from an SO(5)-symmetric disordered phase at large coupling. The critical point becomes multicritical upon the inclusion of terms that break the SO(5) symmetry down to $\mathrm{U}(1) \times \mathrm{SU}(2)$, relevant for the deconfined phase transition between Néel antiferromagnetic and valence-bond-solid orders in quantum magnets. While the precise nature of the disordered phase in the thermodynamic limit remains to be determined, we argue that it is neither conformal nor trivially gapped, akin to a chiral quantum spin liquid with a small gap. Our finding of a multicritical point in the phase diagram of the SO(5) nonlinear sigma model with Wess-Zumino-Witten term resolves the long-standing open question of its global structure, and our QMC framework opens a new avenue for systematic studies of projected Hamiltonians, ranging from correlated flat bands to fractional quantum (anomalous) Hall systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03568"></a>
### [Optimal Navigation in Stochastic and Disordered Gridworlds](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03568v1)

**Authors:** Kévin Bilaï Biloa, Olivier Pierre-Louis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03568v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03568_figures/2605.03568_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Gridworld navigation model. Left: a particle diffuses, and reaches the target state indicated by the star. Center: the policy ϕ is defined in each state. Right: we use a simple barrier-passing model. The policy increases the rate along the force and decreases them in the opposite direction. Black sites are traps with a deeper energy well.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03568_figures/2605.03568_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Optimal policies in gridworld with traps. Results obtained via DP on a 65 × 65 lattice. Trap depth: ∆E/kBT = 10. Colored regions denote sites with a unique optimal orientation; white regions indicate a degenerate policy. (a) Homogeneous environment. (b) Single trap. (c) Four traps. (d) 1% trap concentration cd = 0.01. (e) 10%, cd = 0.1. (f) 50%, cd = 0.5.</figcaption>

In (a,b,c), each point is obtained from an average over 2000 disorder realizations with at least one defect.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper quantifies how random obstacles change the best path a particle can take to reach a target. It reveals that the impact of disorder on navigation is not linear, peaking at surprisingly low trap concentrations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how spatial disorder, specifically randomly distributed traps, reshapes optimal navigation policies in stochastic environments.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered a non-monotonic dependence of the policy change on trap concentration, with a pronounced maximum occurring unexpectedly at low trap concentrations in the fluctuation-dominated regime.

**Method.** The authors use dynamic programming (value iteration) to solve the Bellman optimality equation and apply the Central Limit Theorem to derive analytical predictions for the density of change.

**Model / system.** A Brownian particle performing a Markovian continuous-time random walk on a two-dimensional square lattice (gridworld) with a local driving force and randomly distributed potential traps.

**Key observables.** Mean First-Passage Time (MFPT) and the density of change (rho_ss) derived from Kullback-Leibler divergence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trap concentration (c_d), trap strength (R), and the ratio of driving force to thermal energy (Fd/kBT).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The process is assumed to be Markovian on a square lattice, and when multiple optimal actions exist due to degeneracy, the choice is made randomly with equal probability.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the gridworld model and trap mechanism; Figure 2 shows spatial maps of optimal policies in homogeneous vs. disordered environments; Figure 3 displays spatial maps of the density of change for different force strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the navigation problem, defines the random trap model and the metric for policy change, presents numerical results via dynamic programming, provides analytical derivations using the Central Limit Theorem, and concludes with a discussion of the results.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Navigation in complex and noisy environments is a key issue in diverse fields from biology to engineering. Despite extensive progress in numerical optimization methods for computing navigation policies, insights into how disorder reshapes optimal navigation remain elusive. To address this question, we investigate the navigation of a Brownian particle in a disordered energy landscape, modeled as a lattice with randomly distributed traps. Using dynamic programming, we compute the optimal navigation policies that minimize the mean first-passage time to a target site. To quantify the impact of disorder, we introduce a density of change from a Kullback-Leibler divergence, which captures how the optimal policy is reshaped by either the presence of disorder or the knowledge of its configuration. Our results reveal a non-monotonic dependence of the change of the policy on trap concentration, with a pronounced maximum. In the fluctuation-dominated regime where the navigation bias is weak, we derive an analytical expression for the density of change, and demonstrate that the maximum occurs unexpectedly at low trap concentrations.

</details>

### [Plasmons in Holographic Ersatz Fermi Liquids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03865v1)

**Authors:** Eli Ismailov, Ulf Gran, Eric Nilsson

**Summary.** This paper develops a holographic framework for 'Ersatz Fermi Liquids' that successfully incorporates Luttinger's theorem through an emergent loop-group symmetry. It demonstrates that including long-range Coulomb interactions leads to the emergence of a damped plasmon mode whose frequency scales correctly with the Fermi surface volume. This provides a consistent way to model collective charge dynamics in strongly correlated systems without needing well-defined quasiparticles.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the failure of conventional holographic models to satisfy Luttinger's theorem and seeks to model the collective charge dynamics (plasmons) in 'Ersatz Fermi Liquids' with long-range Coulomb interactions.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a holographic model using LU(1) symmetry correctly implements Luttinger's theorem and predicts a damped, gapped plasmon mode with a plasma frequency scaling as the square root of the Fermi momentum.

**Method.** The study uses a 5D Maxwell-Chern-Simons theory in an AdS-Schwarzschild background, employing linear response theory, Fourier decomposition of the loop group, and a pseudo-spectral numerical method.

**Model / system.** A holographic 'Ersatz Fermi Liquid' (EFL) characterized by an emergent LU(1) symmetry and a 't Hooft anomaly, modeled via a 5D Maxwell-Chern-Simons theory in a static AdS-Schwarzschild black hole background.

**Key observables.** Density-density correlation function, plasma frequency, electromagnetic current-current response, and integrated spectral weight of Fourier modes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermi momentum (kF), anomaly coefficient (m), Fermi velocity (vF), Hawking temperature (T), and the gauge coupling (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes an infrared effective theory limit, a truncated Fourier expansion of the loop group, and a specific AdS-Schwarzschild geometry representing a finite-temperature state.

**Figures summary.** Figure 4 shows the scaling of plasma frequency with kF; Figure 5 displays the gapped plasmon mode in the density-density correlation function; Figure 6 shows frequency slices of the response; Figure 7 compares spectral weight decay for different boundary conditions; Figure 8 demonstrates the exponential convergence of Chebyshev coefficients.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Luttinger's theorem in holography, defines the LU(1) symmetry and the Maxwell-Chern-Simons holographic model, details the numerical implementation and boundary conditions, presents the results for plasmon scaling and damping, and concludes with a discussion on mode decay and convergence.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We solve an infrared effective holographic model of a non-Fermi liquid at finite temperature that satisfies Luttinger's theorem and incorporates long-range Coulomb interactions. Motivated by the absence of a Luttinger-counting Fermi surface in standard Reissner-Nordstrom holographic metals, we consider a Maxwell-Chern-Simons theory in a static anti-de Sitter-Schwarzschild background, coupled to an LU(1) gauge field rather than a conventional U(1) gauge field. By an appropriate choice of boundary conditions, we obtain a damped collective plasmon mode whose plasma frequency scales as predicted by Luttinger's theorem. We further analyze the density-density correlator in the absence of long-range Coulomb interactions and identify a contribution consistent with a Lindhard-like continuum.

</details>

**Authors:** Yu-Ting Lee, Samuel Yen-Chi Chen, Fu-Chieh Chang

Conversely, the Intra-Option Policies are |Ω| independent networks. **Summary.** This paper explores integrating variational quantum circuits into hierarchical reinforcement learning to achieve higher parameter efficiency. It finds that while quantum feature extractors can significantly outperform classical models with fewer parameters, using quantum circuits for value estimation currently acts as a performance bottleneck.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the advantages of Quantum Reinforcement Learning, such as parameter efficiency and expressivity, extend to Hierarchical Reinforcement Learning (HRL) using the option-critic architecture.

**Main result.** A hybrid agent using a quantum feature extractor outperforms classical baselines while reducing trainable parameters by up to 66%, though using quantum circuits for option-value estimation creates a performance bottleneck.

**Method.** The authors develop a hybrid quantum-classical option-critic agent that replaces specific classical components with Variational Quantum Circuits (VQCs) using data re-uploading and hardware-efficient ansatz.

**Model / system.** The study uses a hybrid reinforcement learning framework applied to classical Gymnasium environments, specifically the CartPole and Acrobot tasks, utilizing VQCs for feature extraction, policies, and value functions.

**Key observables.** Expectation values of Pauli-Z operators, policy entropy, actor loss, and critic loss.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth, learnable input-scaling parameters (lambda), CNOT entanglement, and the number of options.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that classical state observations can be effectively mapped to quantum states via unitary encoding and does not account for hardware noise in the simulations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the hybrid RL paradigm; Figure 2 details the option-critic architecture; Figure 3 illustrates the VQC structure; Figure 6 shows learning curves; Figure 8 provides a diagnostic analysis of the quantum critic failure; Figure 10 shows ablation studies on circuit depth and entanglement.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of quantum HRL, details the hybrid option-critic architecture and VQC implementation, presents experimental results on CartPole and Acrobot, conducts ablation studies on circuit parameters, and discusses identified bottlenecks and future directions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reinforcement learning is one of the most challenging learning paradigms where efficacy and efficiency gains are extremely valuable. Hierarchical reinforcement learning is a variant that leverages temporal abstraction to structure decision-making. While parametrized quantum computations have shown success in non-hierarchical reinforcement learning, whether these advantages adapt to hierarchical decision-making remains a critical open question. In this work, we develop a hybrid hierarchical agent based on the option-critic architecture. This hybrid agent substitutes classical components with variational quantum circuits for feature extractors, option-value functions, termination functions, and intra-option policies. Evaluated on standard benchmarking environments, results show that a hybrid agent utilizing a quantum feature extractor can outperform classical baselines while saving up to 66\% trainable parameters. We also identify an architectural bottleneck that quantum option-value estimation severely degrades performance. Further ablation studies reveal how architectural choices of the quantum circuits affect performance. Our work establishes design principles for parameter-efficient hybrid hierarchical agents.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03987"></a>
### [Quantum Metric Localization and Quantum Metric Protection](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03987v1)

**Authors:** Wen-Bo Dai, Jinchao Zhao, Shuai A. Chen, K. T. Law

(a) In a trivial band (¯gxx → 0), optimally localized Wannier states can collapse to the atomic limit. The spatial density overlap between adjacent sites vanishes, shutting down disorder-induced hopping and diffusion (D →0). (b) The nontrivial quantum metric (¯gxx &gt; 0) enforces a lower bound on the spatial spread of the Wannier states, guarantees a finite overlap between the Wannier states, driving a diffusion constant D ∝ Γ¯gxx. In the regime where disorder exceeds the cen- tral bandwidth α = 0.015∆, trivial bands cross over into an atomic insulating limit. **Summary.** This paper identifies a new regime of localization where the localization length is determined by the intrinsic quantum geometry of a band rather than the disorder strength. It demonstrates that the quantum metric provides a 'protection' mechanism that keeps the localization length constant even as disorder increases, until the disorder becomes large enough to mix different bands.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates how the quantum metric of an isolated band influences electronic localization in the presence of disorder, specifically challenging the standard Anderson localization paradigm.

**Main result.** The authors discover a 'quantum metric localization' regime where the localization length becomes pinned at a plateau of approximately twice the quantum metric length, providing a form of 'quantum metric protection' against disorder.

**Method.** The study combines numerical Transfer Matrix Method simulations with analytical approaches, including a physical picture based on Wannier functions and a supersymmetric (SUSY) non-linear sigma model.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a 1D modified Lieb lattice (applicable to electronic, photonic, and acoustic systems) featuring a central band separated from other bands by a gap $\Delta$, subjected to on-site Anderson disorder.

**Key observables.** Localization length ($\xi$), quantum metric length ($l_{QM}$), and the ratio $\xi/l_{QM}$.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Disorder strength ($\Gamma$), band gap ($\Delta$), bandwidth ($4t$), and quantum metric length ($l_{QM}$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes an isolated band where interband mixing is negligible ($\Delta \gg \Gamma$) and utilizes a gradient expansion in the effective field theory.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the 1D Lieb lattice, energy spectrum, and the emergence of the localization length plateau; Fig 3 illustrates the crossover between Anderson and quantum metric localization and the failure of protection at large $\Gamma$; Fig 4 demonstrates the robustness of the plateau in spinful systems.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of localization in flat bands, presents numerical evidence of the localization plateau in a 1D Lieb lattice, provides a physical explanation via Wannier functions, uses a SUSY non-linear sigma model to derive the universal scaling $\xi \approx 2l_{QM}$, explores the breakdown of protection via interband mixing, and tests robustness in multiband spinful systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The study of disorder effects in electronic systems is one of the central themes in physics. It is well established that in the Anderson localization regime, the localization length of electrons decreases monotonically as the disorder strength increases. Here, we demonstrate that the conventional Anderson localization paradigm fails completely in describing an isolated band with quantum metric, where the quantum metric of the band defines a length scale called the quantum metric length. For an isolated band with a finite bandwidth separated from other bands by a band gap $Δ$, weak disorder results in conventional Anderson localization behavior. However, as the disorder increases, the localization length ceases to decrease and becomes pinned at a value approximately twice the quantum metric length, forming a localization length plateau. We term the regime within this localization length plateau as the quantum metric localization regime. Remarkably, the localization length does not deviate from the plateau until the disorder strength far exceeds $Δ$. We refer to this strong protection against disorder, characterized by the quantum metric length, as quantum metric protection. In this work, we first numerically demonstrate quantum metric localization using a 1D Lieb lattice. We then provide a simple physical picture based on the properties of Wannier functions to explain the origin of the localization length plateau. A supersymmetric field theory approach explains why the localization length is approximately twice the quantum metric length and captures the crossover from Anderson localization to quantum metric localization. Our conclusions are broadly applicable to disordered electronic, photonic, and acoustic systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03467"></a>
### [Quantum Resource Estimation for Minimising Energy Grid Losses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03467v1)

**Authors:** Camille de Valk, Milou van Nederveen, Koen Reerink, Werner van Westering  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03467v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03467_figures/2605.03467_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: The MV network in Arnhem, shown with its graph topology. The network is decomposed into biconnected components 𝐺𝐶(pink dashed lines) and their topological minors 𝐺0 (blue lines). Normal nodes appear as black dots, component roots of each 𝐺0 as white dots, the power supply as a grey dot. Only nodes with known coordinates are plotted.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03467_figures/2605.03467_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Resource estimation results for implementing one optimisation layer 𝑈𝑜𝑝𝑡. Each marker represents a qubit technology configuration. The shaded region highlights the achievable trade-off region between runtime and required physical qubits. Microsoft’s Quantum Resource Estimator determines surface code distance automatically based on the logical requirements. It should be noted that these are not the resources required for a full optimisation pipeline, which would mean multiple applications of 𝑈𝑜𝑝𝑡and adding read-out time.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper evaluates the feasibility of using gate-based quantum computing to optimize electricity grid topologies to minimize power losses. By using a higher-order optimization formulation (HUBO), the authors provide a resource estimation that shows how network complexity directly drives the required quantum hardware scale.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Estimating the quantum computational resources (qubits and runtime) required to solve the NP-hard Distribution Network Reconfiguration (DNR) problem for minimizing power losses in electricity grids.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that constructing HUBO formulations for real-world networks is feasible, but resource requirements scale extremely rapidly with network size and structural complexity (connectivity and cyclicity).

**Method.** The authors formulate the DNR problem as a Higher-Order Unconstrained Binary Optimization (HUBO) problem and use the Microsoft Quantum Resource Estimator to perform Quantum Resource Estimation (QRE) for various gate-based and Majorana qubit architectures.

**Model / system.** The system is a medium-voltage electricity distribution network modeled as a directed graph. The optimization is formulated via a Pauli Hamiltonian (H_HUBO) representing a weighted sum of penalty terms (for vertex, edge, and cycle constraints) and an objective term for power losses.

**Key observables.** Number of logical qubits, number of rotation gates, number of interaction terms, and estimated execution runtime.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network connectivity, cyclicity, number of nodes/edges, and the number of optimization layers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the use of the surface code for error correction and focuses on the resources for a single optimization layer rather than a full multi-layer pipeline.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the network graph topology and decomposition into biconnected components; Figure 2 shows the trade-off between runtime and physical qubit count for different qubit technologies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the DNR problem, presents the HUBO formulation and graph reduction techniques, details the quantum resource estimation methodology, analyzes the scaling of resources relative to network topology, and discusses future implications for large-scale grids.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Distribution network reconfiguration (DNR) can minimise power losses by identifying the optimal topology of the electricity grid. Determining the minimum loss configuration is NP-hard, and classical optimisation methods struggle to scale to real-world distribution grids. This paper explores the use of gate-based quantum computing to solve DNR for power loss reduction. We formulate DNR as a higher-order unconstrained binary optimisation (HUBO) problem, avoiding the need for auxiliary variables, thereby reducing the required number of qubits. This is applied to a real medium voltage (MV) network operated by Alliander, a Dutch distribution system operator (DSO). For each biconnected component in the network graph, we construct the corresponding HUBO, derive the cost and mixer operators, and determine the number of required logical qubits and rotation gates. These are then mapped to physical qubits and execution time estimates using quantum resource estimation (QRE). The results suggest that the quantum resource requirements depend not only on component size but also on structural characteristics such as connectivity and cyclicity. Overall, the novelty of this work lies in directly framing the optimisation problem as a HUBO, applying it to real-world MV networks, and performing a QRE to assess future feasibility.

</details>

### [Quasinormal modes and continuum response of de Sitter black holes via complex scaling method](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03277v1)

**Summary.** This paper develops a unified spectral framework using the complex scaling method to study both the discrete resonances and the continuous spectrum of de Sitter black holes. It reveals how the cosmological constant influences the spectral density and shows that the lowest quasinormal mode dominates the continuum response. The method is also shown to be applicable to more complex, higher-dimensional, and string-inspired black hole models.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to extend the complex scaling method (CSM) to Schwarzschild-de Sitter spacetimes to investigate how a non-zero cosmological constant modifies both quasinormal-mode poles and the continuum spectral response.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that CSM provides a unified framework for treating QNM poles and the rotated continuum, showing that the lowest QNM pole is the primary driver of the continuum level density structure and that increasing the cosmological constant shifts these features toward lower frequencies.

**Method.** The complex scaling method (CSM) is applied to Regge-Wheeler-type perturbation equations, using a numerical variational approach with a polynomial times real-range Gaussian basis expansion.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on four-dimensional Schwarzschild-de Sitter black holes, including scalar, electromagnetic, and gravitational perturbations, as well as extensions to higher-dimensional (5D) and string-inspired (MSW) black hole models.

**Key observables.** Quasinormal mode (QNM) frequencies, continuum level density (CLD), transmission phase, and greybody factors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cosmological constant (Lambda), spin (s = 0, 1, 2), angular momentum (l = 2, 3), and the scaling angle (theta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the weakly curved regime (small Lambda) and assumes the validity of the Regge-Wheeler-type master equations in the de Sitter background.

**Figures summary.** Plots of the complex frequency plane showing discretized continua and QNM candidates; numerical tables of QNM frequencies; and series of plots showing the continuum level density (CLD) and its partial reconstruction using the lowest QNM poles.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the mathematical framework of CSM in dS spacetimes, proceeds to numerical implementation and validation against known Schwarzschild results, analyzes the dependence of QNMs and CLD on the cosmological constant, and concludes with extensions to higher-dimensional and coupled-channel systems.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We apply the complex scaling method to black-hole perturbations in four-dimensional Schwarzschild--de~Sitter (dS) spacetimes. The method converts the outgoing-wave boundary-value problem into a non-Hermitian spectral problem and enables quasinormal-mode poles and the rotated continuum to be treated in a common framework. We focus in particular on the continuum level density, which characterizes the continuum response beyond isolated quasinormal-mode frequencies. Using Regge--Wheeler-type perturbation equations for scalar, electromagnetic, and gravitational fields, we investigate how a nonzero cosmological constant modifies the pole and continuum sectors. We also discuss a possible extension to string-inspired coupled-channel systems, and illustrate that higher-dimensional dS black holes can be treated within the same framework, at least in tensor- and vector-type sectors. Our results indicate that complex scaling offers a useful spectral framework for analyzing both quasinormal modes and continuum response in black-hole physics.

</details>

### [Reducing Postselection Overhead in Magic-State Cultivation by In-Patch Multiplexing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03616v1)

**Authors:** Dongmin Kim, Jeonggeun Seo, Aniket Patra, Youngsun Han

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

(b) In magic-state cultivation, a single local magic state is grown through staged operations, such as injection, cultivation, and escape, to obtain a high-fidelity logical magic state within a larger logical patch.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03616_figures/2605.03616_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

During injection, four local color-code regions are initialized within a common logical patch. During cultivation, the local regions are grown from d = 3 to d = 5 and checked in parallel. After a surviving candidate is selected, the protocol returns to the standard single escape path through grafting, producing the final d = 15 grafted-code logical patch. Thus, multiplexing is applied only to the early injection-and-cultivation stages, while the final escape stage remains a single continuation path.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

(b) Explicit two-dimensional embedding of the four local code patches within the distance-15 logical patch. The four sites implement the same local growth primitive and are arranged as symmetry-related place- ments with...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

Four local sites (C1–C4) begin injection and cultivation in parallel within a single logical patch. Sites that trigger an error-detection event are reset and removed from further advancement, while the surviving sites form the candidate set for continuation. One candidate is selected for the final escape stage, and the remaining sites stay idle for the rest of the shot.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a multiplexing strategy for magic-state cultivation that utilizes idle space within a logical patch to run multiple cultivation trials in parallel. By doing so, it significantly reduces the frequency of discarded shots due to early-stage errors, thereby lowering the computational overhead for implementing non-Clifford gates in fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** High postselection overhead in Magic-State Cultivation (MSC) protocols leads to high discard rates and inefficient use of spacetime volume in fault-tolerant quantum computing.

**Main result.** The proposed in-patch multiplexing scheme significantly reduces the expected number of attempts required for successful magic-state preparation, with reductions of up to 78.69% in full-cycle attempts at a physical error rate of 2e-3.

**Method.** The authors propose an in-patch multiplexing protocol that embeds multiple local cultivation sites within a single large logical patch, using a 'grafting' process to forward successful candidates to a standard escape stage.

**Model / system.** The protocol is modeled within a fault-tolerant quantum error correction framework using color codes and surface codes, subject to a uniform depolarizing noise model including idle noise.

**Key observables.** Injection-and-cultivation (IC) expected attempts, full-cycle expected attempts, logical error rate, and the decoder-based gap threshold.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Physical error rate (p = 2e-3), local cultivation distances (d1 = 3, 5), final logical distance (d2 = 15), and the decoder gap threshold (G).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The efficiency gain relies on an independent-site approximation, which assumes no correlation between the multiplexed sites (e.g., no crosstalk).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares distillation vs. cultivation; Figure 2 shows resource underutilization in single-site MSC; Figure 3 illustrates the multiplexed protocol structure; Figure 4 shows the 2D lattice embedding of the four sites; Figure 5 shows the end-to-end execution flow; Figure 11 shows cumulative fractions of correct and erroneous shots.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of MSC overhead, presents the proposed in-patch multiplexing architecture, details the protocol stages (injection, cultivation, and escape), provides numerical results comparing the scheme to a single-site baseline, and discusses the scaling of benefits and limitations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fault-tolerant quantum computing requires high-fidelity logical magic states for implementing non-Clifford operations. Magic-state cultivation provides a lower-overhead route to logical magic-state preparation, but its efficiency is limited by postselection loss during the early injection-and-cultivation stages. In this work, we propose an in-patch multiplexing scheme that uses early-stage idle resources within a single logical patch to create multiple local cultivation opportunities. A candidate that passes the early stages is forwarded to the standard escape pathway, while the escape stage and the decoder-based acceptance procedure are kept identical to those of the single-site baseline. Under a uniform depolarizing noise model with idle noise, the proposed protocol substantially reduces the injection-and-cultivation discard rate and the expected number of attempts required to obtain an accepted early-stage candidate. At a physical error rate of \(p=2\times10^{-3}\), the injection-and-cultivation expected attempts are reduced by \(45.46\%\) for \(d_1=3\) and by \(72.91\%\) for \(d_1=5\), relative to the single-site MSC baseline. In the direct full-cycle evaluation including escape, the expected attempts per kept logical output are further reduced by \(49.04\%\) for \(d_1=3\) and by \(78.69\%\) for \(d_1=5\) at the same physical error rate. The full-cycle cost curves are shifted toward smaller expected attempts, while the final logical-error behavior remains governed by the escape-stage gap threshold. These results show that in-patch multiplexing can reduce postselection overhead while preserving the standard magic-state cultivation framework.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides an analytical framework for the bosonization of spin-S Kitaev-type chains using renormalization group theory. It demonstrates that the effects of broken emergent symmetries vanish in the large-S limit and provides specific predictions for the coefficients that govern magnetic ordering.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the bosonization coefficients for spin-S Kitaev-Gamma and Kitaev-Heisenberg-Gamma chains and understanding how the breaking of emergent continuous symmetries scales with spin S.

**Main result.** The study finds that symmetry-breaking effects in bosonization formulas scale as 1/S in the large-S limit and identifies ten independent bosonization coefficients in the Kitaev-Heisenberg-Gamma model, five of which are independent of Heisenberg coupling to linear order.

**Method.** Renormalization group (RG) analysis combined with one-loop diagrammatic calculations and validation via Density Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) numerical simulations.

**Model / system.** One-dimensional spin-S Kitaev-Gamma and Kitaev-Heisenberg-Gamma chains, which serve as quasi-1D models for 2D Kitaev materials like alpha-RuCl3.

**Key observables.** Bosonization coefficients (C and D), spin correlation functions, and scaling of symmetry-breaking terms.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin quantum number S (half-odd-integer), Kitaev coupling (K), Gamma interaction (Gamma), Heisenberg exchange (J), and the parameter delta-Gamma.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Small delta-Gamma limit, large-S limit for scaling relations, and neglecting higher-order terms in delta-Gamma and J.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the ratio of bosonization coefficients as a function of parameter phi; Figure 3 and 4 show diagrammatic representations of the RG vertex and Feynman diagrams for scaling field renormalization; Figure 5 shows log-log plots of spin correlation components.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the spin-S Kitaev models, performs a microscopic derivation using a fermionic representation, applies RG analysis to calculate the renormalization of scaling fields, validates results with DMRG, and discusses the large-S scaling behavior.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Based on a renormalization group (RG) analysis, we study the bosonization formulas in spin-S Kitaev-Gamma and Kitaev-Heisenberg-Gamma chains in the (K < 0, Gamma > 0, J > 0) parameter region, where S is a half-odd integer. We find that the effects associated with the breaking of emergent continuous symmetries in bosonization formulas scale as 1/S in the large-S limit, which is in qualitative agreement with DMRG numerical results for Kitaev-Gamma chains. In Kitaev-Heisenberg-Gamma chains, symmetry analysis reveals ten independent bosonization coefficients, five of which are predicted by the RG analysis to have no dependence on the Heisenberg coupling up to linear order. Our work may offer valuable input for determining magnetic ordering tendencies in two-dimensional Kitaev spin models within a quasi-one-dimensional approach.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the topology of a wormhole and the breaking of Lorentz symmetry together influence the quantum energy levels of scalar fields. It demonstrates that the wormhole geometry acts as a regulator that removes singularities and creates a discrete, predictable energy spectrum.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the quantum dynamics and spectral properties of scalar bosonic oscillator fields propagating in a (3+1)-dimensional Lorentz-violating wormhole spacetime.

**Main result.** The researchers found that the wormhole geometry eliminates centrifugal singularities and produces a discrete energy spectrum with particle-antiparticle symmetry, where the spectrum is regulated by curvature and Lorentz-violation strength.

**Method.** The radial Klein-Gordon equation is mapped to a 1D Schrödinger-type equation and solved using the confluent Heun differential equation framework to find conditionally exact solutions.

**Model / system.** A 4D traversable, static, spherically symmetric Lorentz-violating wormhole with a nonminimally coupled vector background that generates an effective KG-oscillator interaction.

**Key observables.** Energy eigenvalues (energy spectrum), effective potential, and particle-antiparticle symmetry.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lorentz-violation parameter (zeta), wormhole throat radius (a), oscillator frequency (Omega), and angular momentum quantum number (l).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system assumes a specific constant-lapse wormhole metric and requires specific parametric constraints (truncation conditions) for the existence of bound states.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the effective potential is regular at the throat; Figure 2 displays the relativistic energy spectrum; Figure 3 illustrates the oscillator frequency as a function of LV deformation and angular momentum.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LV wormhole geometry, formulates the Klein-Gordon equation with a vector background, maps the radial equation to a Schrödinger-type form, solves the resulting confluent Heun problem, and analyzes the resulting energy spectrum and parameter dependencies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the quantum dynamics of scalar bosonic oscillator fields propagating in a (3+1)-dimensional Lorentz-violating (LV) wormhole spacetime within a modified gravity framework. The underlying geometry, characterized by a smooth minimal-radius throat and a globally regular redshift sector, induces nontrivial curvature effects that significantly modify the spectral properties of the Klein-Gordon (KG) field. The field dynamics are formulated in the presence of a nonminimally coupled vector background of the form $\mathcal{F}_μ=(\mathcal{F}_t(x),0,0,0)$, which, under the physically motivated ansatz $\mathcal{F}_t(x)=Ω\, r(x)$, generates an effective KG-oscillator interaction intrinsically encoded by the wormhole geometry. The resulting effective potential is regular and finite at the throat, eliminating centrifugal singularities and ensuring globally well-defined propagation across the minimal-radius region. The spectral problem reduces to a confluent Heun structure, leading to conditionally exact solutions and a discrete energy spectrum governed by curvature, Lorentz-violation strength, and oscillator frequency. The associated eigenvalue structure exhibits a relativistic particle-antiparticle symmetry with curvature-induced deformation and parameter-dependent confinement. Our results demonstrate that LV wormhole spacetimes act as effective dispersive quantum gravitational media, in which spacetime topology and spontaneous Lorentz symmetry breaking jointly regulate confinement, spectral quantization, and the global evolution of scalar bosonic modes.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03981"></a>
### [Selecting optimal unrestricted Hartree-Fock trial wavefunctions for phaseless auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo: Accuracy and limitations in modeling three iron-sulfur clusters](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03981v1)

**Authors:** Don Danilov, Brad Ganoe, Leon Otis, Zhi Gong, Zixiang Lu, James Shee  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03981v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03981_figures/2605.03981_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Cluster geometry (left), idealized spin configuration (middle), and corresponding reference spin density metric (right) for [2Fe–2S]2+.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03981_figures/2605.03981_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Same as Figure 1 but for [4Fe–4S]2+ (top) and [4Fe–4S]4+ (bottom) states.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03981_figures/2605.03981_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Deviations of the ph-AFQMC-UHF and UCCSD(T) energies from the DMRG reference, as a function of the deviation of the UHF trial/reference energy from DMRG.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03981_figures/2605.03981_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Figure 4: ph-AFQMC-UHF and UCCSD(T) energies as a function of the UHF trial/reference energy. The three reference theoretical best estimates are ERef[++– –] = −327.247062 ± 0.000984, ERef[+–+–]= −327.245289 ± 0.000733, ERef[+– –+]= −327.248199 ± 0.001820 from Zhai et al,32 wherein the 3 solutions are referred to as BS1, BS2, and BS3 respectively (the error bars are omitted for clarity).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03981_figures/2605.03981_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Deviations of the ph-AFQMC-UHF and UCCSD(T) energies from the theoretical best estimate32</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how the choice of mean-field trial wavefunctions affects the accuracy of ph-AFQMC in transition metal clusters. It demonstrates that while energy-minimizing trials are not always best, selecting trials that preserve physical symmetries can yield surprisingly accurate results despite significant sampling bias.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insight into the systematic errors and sampling pathologies (like overcorrelation) that occur when using mean-field importance functions in many-body Monte Carlo simulations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining how to select optimal Unrestricted Hartree-Fock (UHF) trial wavefunctions for phaseless auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo (ph-AFQMC) to minimize bias in strongly correlated systems.

**Main result.** The study finds that choosing trial wavefunctions based on physical symmetries and chemical properties (like spin density) is more effective than minimizing variational energy, though the trial states remain suboptimal importance functions that can lead to sampling bias.

**Method.** Phaseless auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo (ph-AFQMC) benchmarked against DMRG, FCI, and CCSD(T) using spin-density metrics to evaluate UHF trial states.

**Model / system.** Three iron-sulfur (Fe-S) active space models ([2Fe-2S]2+, [4Fe-4S]2+, and [4Fe-4S]4+) and a stretched helium dimer cation (He2+) used as an analogy for sampling bias.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energies, atomic spin densities, Mulliken spin density, Spin Density Metric (SDM), overlap between stochastic and trial wavefunctions, and local energy fluctuations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Imaginary-time step (0.005), walker populations (1024-2048), and Cholesky decomposition threshold (10^-12).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The phaseless constraint is assumed to be unbiased if the trial wavefunction is an exact eigenstate; the study explores the breakdown of this assumption.

**Figures summary.** The figures show ph-AFQMC energy errors relative to DMRG/FCI as a function of the Spin Density Metric, energy evolution over imaginary time, and the decay of wavefunction overlap.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the ph-AFQMC problem, presents investigations of specific Fe-S clusters, uses a He2+ dissociation model to explain the mechanism of sampling bias, and concludes with implications for trial wavefunction design.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Phaseless auxiliary-field quantum Monte Carlo (ph-AFQMC) has emerged as a promising electronic structure method for correlated electronic systems. However, the quality of its predictions depends critically on the choice of trial wavefunction, and it is not obvious how to make an optimal choice especially for strongly correlated states of large systems. Mean-field wavefunctions are compelling trial wavefunction candidates as they map directly to chemical concepts and can be obtained with $O(N^4)$ cost. Yet in the strongly correlated regime one faces a symmetry dilemma and the existence of multiple nearly-degenerate solutions. In this work we investigate active space models of [2Fe-2S]$^{2+}$, mixed-valent [4Fe-4S]$^{2+}$, and [4Fe-4S]$^{4+}$ and explore the sensitivity of ph-AFQMC to the choice of unrestricted Hartree-Fock trial wavefunction. We find that chemical properties and physical symmetries, rather than the variational energy, ought to guide the choice of mean-field trial for ph-AFQMC (or reference state for coupled cluster models), and show that surprisingly accurate ground-state energies for these systems can be obtained. However, in all cases we find a rapidly vanishing overlap between the stochastic wavefunction and the UHF trial, indicating that the trials are suboptimal importance functions. By analogy to a similar situation in the stretched helium dimer cation, we show how this sampling bias pushes ph-AFQMC towards artificially negative energies, which evidently can be compensated for by the phaseless bias in certain cases.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03545"></a>
### [Shortest Path in Pauli Forest -- An Algorithm for Decomposing Pauli Exponentials to Quantum Circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03545v1)

**Authors:** Lauri Vuorenkoski, Arianne Meijer-van de Griend  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03545v1>

**Summary.** The paper introduces the 'Shortest Path in Pauli Forest' algorithm, which optimizes the synthesis of Pauli exponentials by treating decomposition as a path-finding problem. It achieves significant reductions in gate counts and execution time by being aware of the underlying quantum hardware architecture. This is crucial for making quantum chemistry simulations more viable on noisy, near-term quantum devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently decomposing Pauli exponentials into quantum circuits while minimizing CNOT count, CNOT depth, and runtime on NISQ-era hardware with specific connectivity constraints.

**Main result.** The proposed Shortest Path in Pauli Forest (SPPF) algorithm significantly reduces CNOT count (up to 71%) and runtime (up to 91%) compared to standard methods like Lexicographical Ordering and Qiskit/TKET for both random Pauli strings and molecular ansätze.

**Method.** A heuristic algorithm that models decomposition as a shortest-path problem in a graph of Clifford tableaus, combined with an architecture-aware initial qubit mapping strategy.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on NISQ-era quantum computing architectures with various topologies (Line, Grid, Guadalupe, and IBM Brisbane/Mumbai) and uses UCCSD molecular ansätze (e.g., H2, LiH, H2O) as application benchmarks.

**Key observables.** CNOT count, CNOT depth, and algorithm runtime.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of Pauli gadgets (g), number of qubits (n), and hardware connectivity/topology.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The algorithm assumes Pauli gadgets are mutually commuting (common in Trotterized Hamiltonians) and utilizes a specific Clifford gate set.

**Figures summary.** Figures demonstrate the decomposition of single gadgets on line topologies, illustrate the construction of connectivity trees on grid topologies, and provide comparative performance plots for CNOT count and runtime across different hardware architectures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Pauli exponential decomposition, reviews existing literature, proposes the SPPF algorithm and mapping strategy, presents performance evaluations on random and molecular systems across various topologies, and concludes with a discussion of results.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Decomposing Pauli exponentials efficiently to quantum circuits has been the subject of intense research in recent years. Pauli exponentials are an essential component of many different quantum algorithms. Due to the error-prone nature of current and near term quantum devices, it is crucial that quantum circuits are as compact as possible. Several different types of algorithms have been developed to decompose Pauli exponentials into as short circuits as possible. We propose a novel algorithm for architecture-aware synthesis of Pauli exponentials that also determines the initial qubit placement on the device. We call this the Shortest Path in Pauli Forest algorithm. The results show an improved CNOT count and runtime for both random Pauli exponentials and molecular ansätze.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03854"></a>
### [Space-Time Tradeoffs of Pauli-Based Computation in Distributed qLDPC Architectures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03854v1)

**Authors:** Naphan Benchasattabuse, Michal Hajdušek, Rodney Van Meter  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03854v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03854_figures/2605.03854_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1: Example of a Q-Fly architecture with ﬁve groups, each composed of four computational quantum nodes, an optical switch and a pool of BSAs.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03854_figures/2605.03854_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 3: Partial circuit illustrating the controlled amplitude distribution ladder for the n-th qubit.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03854_figures/2605.03854_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 2: Optimized synthesis of controlled continuous rota- tions. (a) The standard sequential fractional rotation. (b) The temporary-AND compute-uncompute (TACU) gadget, which intercepts multiple controls to bypass exponential angle frac- turing. **Summary.** This paper investigates how to leverage the high-rate capacity of qLDPC codes in a distributed architecture to overcome the sequential bottlenecks of Pauli-based computation. It demonstrates that by using architecture-aware circuit design, one can achieve significant speedups in quantum optimization algorithms compared to traditional surface code approaches.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the space-time tradeoffs in distributed quantum computing (DQC) by addressing the bottleneck of slow inter-node entanglement generation in Pauli-based computation (PBC) architectures.

**Main result.** Large qLDPC code blocks in a Q-Fly architecture outperform the surface code baseline in execution time by up to an order of magnitude for quantum optimization algorithms like QAOA and DQI.

**Method.** The authors use architecture-aware circuit design and analytical resource estimation, comparing custom-designed distributed circuits against a monolithic surface code baseline using the Q-Fly network topology.

**Model / system.** A hierarchical Q-Fly architecture consisting of 64 groups of 12 computational nodes connected via a chordal ring graph, utilizing high-rate qLDPC (bivariate bicycle) codes and photonic interconnects for Bell pair generation.

**Key observables.** Logical cycle counts, execution time (latency), and network operation counts.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Network latency (T_Bell ranging from 2 to 10 cycles), routing diameter (max 3 hops), node capacity (~1,000 physical qubits), and rotation precision (64-bit).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes photonic interconnects mediate the network, nodes have sufficient local magic state factories, and routing is limited to two switch hops to maintain fidelity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the Q-Fly architecture hierarchy; Figure 2 shows the optimized synthesis of controlled rotations using the TACU gadget; Figure 3 illustrates the WDB-based Dicke state construction.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of PBC bottlenecks in DQC, defines the Q-Fly architecture and qLDPC codes, details the construction of architecture-aware subroutines (adders, rotations, fan-out), performs resource estimation for QAOA and DQI algorithms, and concludes with a comparative performance analysis against surface code architectures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Pauli-based computation (PBC) provides a universal framework for executing fault-tolerant quantum algorithms using Pauli measurements and magic states. In monolithic architectures, the serialized nature of PBC directly ties runtime to a circuit's T-gate count, making it slow on metrics like circuit depth. However, in distributed quantum computing (DQC), the primary bottleneck is remote Bell pair generation. We investigate the tradeoff between error-correcting code block size and execution time of PBC within the Q-Fly architecture at intermediate scale, limiting individual node capacities to reflect near-term constraints while supplying abundant network nodes to minimize routing and compilation effects. We find that large qLDPC code blocks outperform the surface code baseline in terms of execution time by up to an order of magnitude when evaluated against quantum optimization algorithms. By moving groups of qubits to free nodes to bypass the sequential bottleneck of PBC, the large-block architecture minimizes network operations and achieves faster overall execution. This demonstrates that PBC is a competitive model in the distributed regime, establishing it as a practical compilation baseline for qLDPC systems before invoking more efficient transversal or homological gates.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how subdominant electron hopping and a strong trigonal crystal field drive complex spin-orbital ordering in MnV2O4. It demonstrates that these interactions stabilize a two-in/two-out magnetic structure with intertwined orbital dipole and quadrupole orders. This work provides a microscopic resolution to the long-standing debate regarding the orbital configuration in this spinel vanadate.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the microscopic origin of the intertwined dipole-quadrupole orbital order and the specific magnetic configuration in the spinel vanadate MnV2O4.

**Main result.** The inclusion of subdominant hopping processes and a strong trigonal crystal field stabilizes a two-in/two-out (2I2O) magnetic configuration with spin canting and ferroquadrupolar orbital order, resolving the debate over the orbital state.

**Method.** The authors combine first-principles DFT calculations with a strong-coupling expansion and mean-field theory using SU(N) coherent states to derive and analyze an effective spin-orbital Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The system is the spinel vanadate MnV2O4, specifically the V3+ ions on a pyrochlore lattice. The model is a multiorbital Kugel-Khomskii-type Hamiltonian including Coulomb interaction, trigonal crystal field, spin-orbit coupling, and electron hopping.

**Key observables.** Spin configurations (AIAO vs 2I2O), orbital dipole and quadrupole moments, orbital occupations, and spin canting angles.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trigonal crystal field splitting (42 meV), spin-orbit coupling strength (27 meV), hopping integrals (t1-t4), and the tuning parameter x for subdominant hopping.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglects intersite quantum fluctuations and single-site spin-orbital entanglement; assumes the validity of the effective low-energy manifold.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1a shows the spinel structure, pyrochlore lattice, and local trigonal axes.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the scientific problem and the physical system, followed by the construction of the multiorbital Hamiltonian using DFT-derived parameters, the derivation of the effective spin-orbital model via perturbation theory, a mean-field analysis of phase evolution, and a conclusion on the stabilized 2I2O state.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Orbitally degenerate systems provide a promising platform for realizing novel quantum phases driven by spin-orbital exchange interactions, as described by the Kugel-Khomskii model. Spinel vanadates, in which orbital degrees of freedom remain active, exhibit structural and magnetic transitions accompanied by orbital ordering, but the nature of the orbital state in MnV$_2$O$_4$ remains under debate. Here, we combine first-principles calculations with an effective spin-orbital model to address this problem. We show that a significant trigonal crystal field is present in high-temperature cubic phase and plays an essential role in determining the low-energy degrees of freedom. Based on the resulting parameters, we construct an effective Hamiltonian beyond the conventional dominant-hopping approximation and demonstrate that subdominant hopping processes strongly modify the spin-orbital exchange interactions. As a result, the system stabilizes a two-in/two-out magnetic configuration featuring spin canting and intertwined dipole-quadrupole orbital order.

</details>
