<a id="paper-2605.03755"></a>
### [Spontaneous Topological Locking and Symmetry Restoration of Meron Lattices in Synthetic Antiferromagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03755v1)

**Authors:** Gülşen Doğan, Ümit Akıncı  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03755v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper uses Monte Carlo simulations to show how weak interlayer coupling in synthetic antiferromagnets can stabilize and restore symmetry in meron-antimeron lattices. It demonstrates that even when global crystal order is lost, local topological synchronization can persist, offering a way to manipulate fractional textures for spintronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the structural symmetry breaking and topological locking of meron-antimeron crystals in synthetic antiferromagnetic (SAF) bilayers under easy-plane anisotropy.

**Main result.** The study finds that ultra-weak interlayer exchange acts as a structural scaffold that restores C4 rotational symmetry and induces lattice compression, even when local topological locking persists after global crystalline order collapses.

**Method.** Large-scale Monte Carlo simulations.

**Model / system.** Synthetic antiferromagnetic (SAF) bilayers subjected to easy-plane magnetic anisotropy, focusing on the formation and stability of meron-antimeron crystals and bimeron dipoles.

**Key observables.** Rotational symmetry (C4 vs C2), inter-core distance, lattice compression, and local topological locking.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Easy-plane magnetic anisotropy, interlayer antiferromagnetic exchange strength, and the uncoupled monolayer limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the Monte Carlo approach for simulating the magnetic textures in the presence of anisotropy and interlayer coupling.

**Paper structure.** The paper investigates the uncoupled monolayer limit, introduces interlayer exchange effects, analyzes the transition from rigid crystals to pre-collapse states, and concludes with the behavior at extreme anisotropy limits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Synthetic antiferromagnets offer a robust platform for stabilizing fractional topological textures, effectively circumventing the limitations of ferromagnetic systems. In this study, we utilize large-scale Monte Carlo simulations to investigate the spontaneous topological locking and structural symmetry restoration of meron-antimeron crystals within SAF bilayers subjected to easy-plane magnetic anisotropy. In the uncoupled monolayer limit, increasing anisotropy induces an extreme core-shrinking effect that physically expands the inter-core distance and triggers a $C_4 \rightarrow C_2$ symmetry breaking. However, the introduction of an ultra-weak interlayer antiferromagnetic exchange acts as an active structural scaffold. For rigid crystals, this coupling strictly enforces vertical synchronization, forming robust antiferromagnetic bimeron dipoles and fully restoring the macroscopic $C_4$ rotational symmetry. Furthermore, in highly expanded, pre-collapse crystals, we observe an anomalous interlayer-induced lattice compression that actively maximizes the exchange energy. At extreme anisotropy limits where macroscopic crystalline order irrecoverably collapses, the bilayer coupling continues to enforce a strict local topological locking of surviving isolated defects. These findings reveal a fundamental decoupling between local vertical synchronization and global structural order, providing a comprehensive theoretical roadmap for stabilizing and manipulating fractional topological textures in beyond-skyrmion spintronic architectures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03879"></a>
### [Thermodynamic phase transitions reveal the resilience structure of urban traffic congestion](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03879v1)

**Authors:** Luis E. Olmos  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03879v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that urban traffic congestion behaves like a thermodynamic phase transition. By defining an effective temperature for cities, the authors can predict which urban infrastructures are prone to sudden, system-wide traffic jams.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the transition of urban traffic from free-flowing to congested states and identifying the underlying drivers of traffic resilience.

**Main result.** City-scale congestion is shown to undergo a nonlinear phase transition analogous to an order-disorder transition, where an effective thermodynamic temperature quantifies infrastructural heterogeneity and predicts congestion fragility.

**Method.** The study applies statistical mechanics frameworks to large-scale urban mobility data, deriving an effective thermodynamic temperature and analyzing a free-energy landscape.

**Model / system.** The system is urban traffic networks across 46 cities in Latin America and the Caribbean, plus 8 additional cities globally, treated as a many-body system where aggregate mobility is the control parameter.

**Key observables.** Aggregate mobility (control parameter), jam extent (order parameter), and an effective thermodynamic temperature.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Aggregate mobility, infrastructural heterogeneity (via effective temperature), and entropy-capacity trade-offs.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paper assumes the traffic transition can be mapped onto a formal thermodynamic phase transition framework.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the phase transition analogy, derives the effective thermodynamic temperature, presents the free-energy landscape, and validates the framework using multi-city empirical data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how cities transition from free-flowing to congested traffic remains a central open problem in urban science. Here we show that city-scale congestion undergoes a reproducible nonlinear transition analogous to an order-disorder phase transition in statistical mechanics, in which aggregate mobility acts as a control parameter and jam extent as a collective order parameter. Crucially, this analogy is not merely formal: we derive and empirically identify an effective thermodynamic temperature with concrete physical meaning, quantifying infrastructural heterogeneity and how broadly a city explores congestion configurations as demand increases. Low-temperature cities are congestion-fragile: small mobility increases trigger sharp, system-wide jam transitions. This framework further reveals that the macroscopic fundamental diagram is an incomplete description of the traffic state: it emerges as a projection of a richer free-energy landscape governed by entropy-capacity trade-offs. Validated across 46 cities in Latin America and the Caribbean and independently confirmed with loop-detector data from 8 cities on three continents, these results establish a physics-based foundation for comparing urban traffic resilience and anticipating congestion regime shifts under changing mobility demand.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03162"></a>
### [Transition Metal Dichalcogenide Excitons in Periodic Electrostatic Potentials: Center-of-Mass Models](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03162v1)

**Authors:** Jose M. Torres-Lopez, Sudipta Kundu, Felipe H. da Jornada, Tony Heinz, Allan H. MacDonald  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03162v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** This paper explores how periodic electrostatic landscapes can be used to engineer the valley properties of excitons in 2D TMDs. It demonstrates that breaking rotational symmetry can lift valley degeneracy and create linear dispersion. These findings suggest a method to stabilize 2D exciton superfluids by suppressing thermal excitations.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery that engineered potentials can create a non-degenerate, linearly dispersing exciton band provides a theoretical pathway to achieving true 2D Bose-Einstein condensation and superfluidity, which is otherwise suppressed by thermal fluctuations in standard 2D systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how periodic electrostatic potentials and reduced rotational symmetry affect the exciton spectra, valley degeneracy, and dispersion in 2D transition-metal dichalcogenide (TMD) semiconductors.

**Main result.** Electrostatic potentials can drive optical valley splitting up to 10 meV and induce valley-selective dispersion, where the lowest exciton band becomes non-degenerate with linear dispersion, potentially enabling 2D Bose condensation.

**Method.** The study uses a center-of-mass model with exact diagonalization, analytical perturbation theory, and a tight-binding/Wannier representation using Gaussian wavefunctions.

**Model / system.** 2D group VI TMD semiconductors subjected to periodic electrostatic potentials (triangular, square, or striped) via the quadratic Stark effect, modeled using a Hamiltonian containing kinetic energy, periodic potential, and electron-hole exchange interaction.

**Key observables.** Optical valley splitting (valley gap), exciton band dispersion (linear vs. quadratic), and optical conductivity/absorption weight.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Potential strength (approx. 1 meV), lattice constant (approx. 50 nm), exchange interaction strength (0.4-0.6 eV nm), and anisotropy parameter (alpha).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The smooth potential limit (weak potential gradients), the local quadratic Stark shift approximation, and the truncation of the exchange interaction to the first shell of reciprocal lattice vectors.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows reciprocal lattice vectors and exciton bands for triangular and square lattices; Figure 2 compares isotropic vs. anisotropic triangular potentials; Figure 3 shows exciton band structures for triangular lattices; Figure 4 compares square and anisotropic potentials.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical system and the center-of-mass model, analyzes the effect of rotational symmetry on valley splitting and dispersion, explores the impact of anisotropy on band stability, and discusses the implications for 2D Bose condensation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Two-dimensional (2D) van-der-Waals materials are a promising platform for exciton state engi- neering. In this paper, we study the properties of excitons in 2D group VI transition-metal dichalco- genide (TMD) semiconductors that are modified by a periodic electrostatic potential through the quadratic Stark effect. Using a model that retains only center-of-mass and valley degrees-of-freedom, we find that electrostatic potentials can drive optical valley splitting up to 10meVs and induce valley selective exciton dispersion. We explain why both properties are sensitive to the rotational symmetry of the electrostatic trapping potential using a combination of numerical results and an- alytical approximations. An important consequence of valley-splitting is that the lowest exciton band is non-degenerate and has a linear dispersion around gamma that is expected to suppress thermal excitations, allowing true Bose condensation and superfluidity of excitons in two space dimensions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03468"></a>
### [Universal qutrit control in asymmetric-top molecules](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03468v1)

**Authors:** Qian-Qian Hong, Zhi-Jian Zheng, Zhe-Jun Zhang, Xin-Xia Jian, Chuan-Cun Shu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03468v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper presents a theoretical method for universal three-level (qutrit) control using the rotational states of asymmetric-top molecules. By using an auxiliary state for phase control, the authors demonstrate high-fidelity gate implementation and provide an analytical tool for designing robust microwave pulse sequences. The study specifically highlights how different gate decomposition strategies impact the system's sensitivity to experimental errors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for AMO physicists as it proposes a new molecular platform for qutrit-based quantum information processing, leveraging the unique $\Delta$-type coupling of asymmetric tops to overcome symmetry constraints found in traditional qubit platforms.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving universal single-qutrit control in asymmetric-top molecules and determining optimal gate decomposition sequences to minimize sensitivity to control errors.

**Main result.** The authors established a theoretical framework for universal qutrit control using asymmetric-top molecules, demonstrating high-fidelity Walsh-Hadamard gates and identifying that phase error sensitivity depends on the specific SU(2) decomposition sequence used.

**Method.** The study uses an analytical protocol combining SU(2) rotations with auxiliary-state-mediated phase operations, employing the first-order Magnus approximation and a multilevel pulse-area theorem to map gate parameters to microwave control fields.

**Model / system.** The system consists of asymmetric-top molecules (specifically 1,2-propanediol) modeled as rigid rotors in the electronic and vibrational ground states, where the qutrit is encoded in three rotational eigenstates coupled via electric-dipole transitions.

**Key observables.** Average gate fidelity, state fidelity, density matrix elements, and error deviations in the generalized Bloch-vector (Gell-Mann) representation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rotational constants (A, B, C), dipole moments (mu_a, mu_b, mu_c), pulse areas (theta), and phase errors (zeta) and amplitude errors (xi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a rigid rotor approximation, the electric-dipole approximation for molecule-field interaction, and uses the first-order Magnus approximation for unitary operator derivation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the qutrit encoding and control scheme; Figure 2 displays gate fidelities; Figure 3 presents the density matrix for the Walsh-Hadamard gate; Figure 4 shows error coefficients and fidelity vs. amplitude error; Figure 5 illustrates the time evolution of error deviations in the Bloch-vector representation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the control framework and molecular platform, derives the analytical pulse-area theorem and gate decomposition, validates the approach with numerical simulations of 1,2-propanediol, performs a detailed error analysis of amplitude and phase perturbations, and concludes with a study of decomposition sequence validity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a theoretical framework for universal single-qutrit control in asymmetric-top molecules, advancing molecular quantum information processing. In this approach, the qutrit is encoded in three rotational eigenstates, with an auxiliary state providing independent phase control within the computational manifold. We explore an analytic protocol for arbitrary single-qutrit gates, combining directly addressable SU(2) rotations with auxiliary-state-mediated phase operations. To support this, we derive a multilevel pulse-area theorem that provides an explicit analytic mapping between gate parameters and control fields, enabling systematic design of high-fidelity microwave pulse sequences. Numerical simulations with 1,2-propanediol confirm the robustness of our approach, achieving Walsh-Hadamard gates with minimal leakage from the computational subspace. We further examine four SU(2) decomposition strategies and find that phase-error sensitivity depends on the decomposition sequence, while amplitude errors propagate along specific coherence pathways. Our results establish asymmetric-top molecules as a viable platform for qutrit-based quantum operations and offer an analytical method for precise quantum control of complex multilevel systems.

</details>

</details>

### [Bogoliubov mode dynamics and non-adiabatic transitions in time-varying condensed media](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03087v1)

**Authors:** A. M. Tishin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03087v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new method for non-destructive testing of materials by measuring the production of phase-conjugate Bogoliubov modes during rapid parameter changes. By using a new dimensionless metric, the author demonstrates that sub-wavelength defects can be detected through phase dynamics even when they are invisible to traditional amplitude-based methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous framework for studying non-equilibrium dynamics, mode-mixing, and the breakdown of the WKB approximation in driven-dissipative bosonic systems, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a metrological framework to detect sub-wavelength structural defects in time-varying condensed media by analyzing non-adiabatic phase-mode redistribution.

**Main result.** The establishment of a universal dimensionless non-adiabaticity parameter (eta) and a scaling law (xi = eta/U) that allows for the detection of defects below the Rayleigh diffraction limit.

**Method.** Numerical simulations using the QuTiP engine with a large-scale bosonic Fock basis and Lindblad Master Equation approach, validated by high-precision RK4 integration.

**Model / system.** Time-varying condensed media such as ultrafast magnetic media, ENZ metamaterials, and magnetoelectric composites, modeled by a second-order oscillator equation with time-dependent frequency and Kerr-type anharmonicity.

**Key observables.** Bogoliubov coefficients (u and v), phase-mode redistribution, spectral asymmetry ratio (|v|^2/|u|^2), and Fock-state occupancy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Non-adiabaticity parameter (eta), stability ratio (xi = eta/U), anharmonicity (U), and critical spatial scale (L_crit).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of a truncated Hilbert space is valid if high-level Fock state occupancy is negligible; the system follows a Euclidean normalization (|u|^2 + |v|^2 approx 1) due to dissipation.

**Figures summary.** Figures show Fock-state occupancy, high-resolution phase-coherence, and thermodynamic audits of the stability map.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of sub-wavelength defect detection, defines the non-adiabaticity metric, classifies material regimes (adiabatic vs. non-adiabatic), provides numerical validation of the scaling law, and concludes with the robustness of the framework against noise.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This study investigates non-adiabatic wave dynamics in condensed media and the transition from adiabatic stability to spectral chaos. We introduce a dimensionless parameter, as a universal metric to quantify phase-mode redistribution at sub-wavelength inhomogeneities. Our framework treats defects as localized sites of adiabaticity violation triggering non-adiabatic parametric excitation of the ground state. Numerical validation in an expanded 50-level bosonic basis demonstrates that the framework accurately distinguishes between adiabatic regimes in ENZ-metamaterials and non-adiabatic transitions in ultrafast magnetic media . We establish a universal scaling law governed by the non-adiabaticity-to-regulation ratio, proving that the proposed metric remains a robust metrological tool for identifying sub-wavelength inhomogeneities across diverse material classes. Computational singularities observed at extreme loads identify the rigorous operational boundaries for coherent mode-mixing. The robustness of the proposed framework is numerically validated, proving the method's reliability for a wide class of non-linear condensed media satisfying the stability criterion. This result provides a rigorous physical justification for the dynamic Hilbert space truncation (effective fermion-like dynamics), ensuring metrological consistency in complex structural environments. These results provide a theoretical foundation for probing ultrafast collective excitations and latent internal stresses, extending structural analysis beyond the traditional diffraction barrier.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03672"></a>
### [Deterministic positioning of circular Bragg gratings using atomic force lithography for high-performance quantum dot light sources](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03672v1)

**Authors:** Sai Abhishikth Dhurjati, Moritz Langer, Yared G. Zena, Ahmad Rahimi, Liesa Raith, Martin Bauer, Frank H. P. Fitzek, Riccardo Bassoli, Caspar Hopfmann  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03672v1>  
**Analysis basis:** abstract only; PDF text extraction failed or PDF unavailable
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

**Summary.** The researchers developed a method using AFM-assisted lithography to precisely position quantum dots within optical microcavities. This technique achieves significant light enhancement and stable polarization, providing a scalable approach for creating high-performance quantum light sources.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and photonic quantum information as it provides a scalable, experimental pathway to creating high-fidelity, deterministic single-photon sources by precisely controlling the light-matter interface.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The random spatial distribution of semiconductor quantum dots (QDs) grown via molecular beam epitaxy prevents deterministic coupling to optical microcavities, which is essential for scalable quantum light sources.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrated an AFM-assisted nano-oxidation lithography technique that enables deterministic QD positioning with a radial displacement of 51(28) nm, achieving a 245-fold photoluminescence enhancement in circular Bragg gratings.

**Method.** Room-temperature atomic force microscopy (AFM)-assisted nano-oxidation lithography is used to position GaAs QDs within asymmetric circular Bragg gratings.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of GaAs semiconductor quantum dots embedded within free-standing asymmetric circular Bragg gratings.

**Key observables.** Photoluminescence enhancement, fine-structure splitting (FSS), polarization-resolved spectroscopy, Stokes parameters, and polarization imbalance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Radial displacement of 51(28) nm, 245-fold photoluminescence enhancement, and polarization imbalance below 5%.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of random QD distribution, describes the AFM-assisted lithography technique, presents the experimental results for QD positioning and photoluminescence enhancement, and concludes with the implications for quantum information technologies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Semiconductor quantum dots (QDs) grown by molecular beam epitaxy are excellent quantum emitters, but their random spatial distribution hinders deterministic coupling to optical microcavities. We demonstrate a room-temperature atomic force microscopy (AFM)-assisted nano-oxidation lithography technique enabling QD positioning with a radial displacement of $51(28)$ nm. Free-standing asymmetric circular Bragg gratings incorporating AFM-positioned GaAs QDs exhibit a $245$-fold photoluminescence enhancement and fine-structure splitting (FSS) comparable to bulk QDs. Polarization-resolved spectroscopy and finite-difference time-domain simulations show robust emission for displacements up to $50$ nm (Stokes parameter $\lvert S \rvert < 0.05$ ). The devices display stable FSS and polarization imbalance below $5 \, \%$ , confirming precise, reproducible alignment and potential for high fidelity devices. This scalable approach enables deterministic integration of high-performance QDs with photonic cavities, advancing practical quantum light sources for quantum information technologies.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.03047"></a>
### [Beam canalization by a non-Abelian gauge field](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03047v1)

**Authors:** Olha Bahrova, Jiahao Ren, Feng Jin, Rui Su, Guillaume Malpuech, Dmitry Solnyshkov  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03047v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<img src="2605.03047_figures/2605.03047_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">

The dashed curves shows the correspond- ing inner band IFCs. The gray curves plotted within the high- lighted sector indicate additional IFCs. (b) Total polariton intensity, obtained from the SE simulation, and normalized along the x axis manifesting pronounced canalization effect strongly enhanced by the present effective non-Abelian gauge field. (c) s3 Stokes vector component.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03047_figures/2605.03047_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. a) The spin projection s3(t) for a single period: nu- merical and analytical solutions. b) The qy(t) projection: nu- merical and analytical solutions. c) The transverse coordinate as a function of time y(t) (numerics, y∗is the oscillation am- plitude). d) Final shift with and without the gauge field as a function of initial wave vector qy(0) (numerics).</figcaption>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper shows that a non-Abelian gauge field can significantly enhance the canalization of light beams in microcavities. By coupling spatial motion to polarization precession, the gauge field provides a Lorentz-like force that suppresses beam spreading. This effect leads to a ten-fold improvement in beam collimation compared to standard predictions.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper explores the emergence of non-Abelian gauge fields and Yang-Mills dynamics in a photonic platform, which is highly relevant to researchers interested in synthetic gauge fields, topological photonics, and the manipulation of pseudospin-orbit coupling.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how a non-Abelian gauge field, arising from the coupling between spatial dynamics and polarization pseudospin precession, affects the canalization (beam collimation) of light in photonic systems.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates a ten-fold enhancement of beam canalization for a Gaussian beam due to the non-Abelian gauge field compared to descriptions based solely on group velocity. It also identifies an anti-symmetric pattern of circular polarization accompanying this effect.

**Method.** The authors use a combination of semi-classical trajectories of wavepacket components and direct numerical solutions of the spinor Schrodinger equation in real space, utilizing a Hamiltonian formalism to derive equations of motion.

**Model / system.** The system consists of planar microcavities where TE-TM splitting and linear birefringence create tilted Dirac points. The dynamics are modeled using a Yang-Mills theory for SU(2) where the system acts as a matter field coupled to an emergent non-Abelian gauge potential.

**Key observables.** Total polariton intensity (beam profile), Stokes vector component s3 (circular polarization degree), pseudospin precession, and Zitterbewegung-like oscillations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** TE-TM splitting strength (beta), optical birefringence (beta0), in-plane wavevector (k), and initial Gaussian beam width.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analytical approach assumes the s3 spin component evolution is equivalent to a nonlinear Duffing oscillator and treats the x-direction wavevector as approximately constant (qx approx 1).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows different isofrequency contour types, beam canalization simulations, and the anti-symmetric s3 pattern. Fig 2 displays the evolution of spin projection, momentum, and transverse coordinates, highlighting the beam shift with and without the gauge field.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of beam canalization via isofrequency contours, presents a mathematical model using non-Abelian gauge fields and Hamiltonian dynamics, provides numerical simulations of beam propagation and spin evolution, and concludes with a comparison of the gauge-field-enhanced effect versus standard group velocity models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hyperbolic and quasi-flat isofrequency contours (IFCs) are used for beam canalization and can be created by tilted Dirac points in photonic systems. Dirac points in microcavities are generated by the combination of transverse-electric/transverse-magnetic splitting and linear birefringence. We show that the canalization is here strongly assisted by the coupling between the spatial dynamics and polarization pseudospin precession. This dynamics is well described analytically and numerically as the action of a non-Abelian gauge field on emergent charges (spin current). We demonstrate a ten-fold enhancement of the canalization for a Gaussian beam by the gauge field, as compared to a description based solely on the group velocity associated with the IFCs.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03876"></a>
### [Remote entropy measurement in coupled quantum dots](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03876v1)

**Authors:** Owen Sheekey, Tim Child, Elena Cornick, Saeed Fallahi, Geoffrey C. Gardner, Michael J. Manfra, Eran Sela, Yaakov Kleeorin, Yigal Meir, Silvia Lüscher, Joshua Folk  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03876v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** The researchers demonstrated a method to measure the entropy of an entire coupled system by monitoring the charge of a single quantum dot. By applying Maxwell relations to temperature-dependent charge measurements, they successfully probed entropy changes in both weakly and strongly coupled regimes. This establishes quantum dots as effective sensors for the thermodynamic properties of nearby mesoscopic circuits.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work demonstrates a way to use a local probe to sense non-local thermodynamic properties, which is highly relevant for monitoring entanglement entropy or topological signatures in open quantum systems and many-body circuits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a method to measure 'remote' entropy—the entropy change of an entire system beyond the local site of an added particle—and testing the ability of a quantum dot to act as an auxiliary sensor for the entropy of a separate, coupled part of a circuit.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that charge measurements on one quantum dot can reveal entropy changes of an entire coupled two-dot system, validating Maxwell relation-based measurements for detecting both microstate-counting and hybridized regimes.

**Method.** Used Maxwell relations to connect entropy change to the temperature dependence of particle number, implemented via rapid reservoir temperature modulation (Joule heating) and charge sensing via a quantum point contact.

**Model / system.** A pair of capacitively coupled GaAs/AlGaAs lateral quantum dots (QD1 and QD2) tunnel-coupled to a shared reservoir, where QD1 is monitored by an adjacent quantum point contact (QPC) charge sensor.

**Key observables.** Change in QPC current (delta I_CS), change in dot occupation (N), and extracted entropy change (delta S_sys).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interdot electrostatic interaction (U12), dot-reservoir coupling (Gamma), temperature (T), and gate voltages.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes no direct interdot tunneling and relies on the validity of Maxwell relations in the mesoscopic regime; the analysis also assumes the ability to isolate spurious signals from the sensor-dot coupling.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device SEM, charging diagrams, and charge sensor signal maps; Fig 3 shows entropy change measurements across transitions and their evolution with coupling; Fig 4 shows entropy change across the (0,1) to (1,0) transition.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the remote sensing problem, describes the experimental GaAs quantum dot platform, details the thermodynamic measurement protocol using Maxwell relations, presents results in the weak-coupling (microstate counting) and strong-coupling (hybridized) regimes, and discusses signal purification techniques.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recent experiments have demonstrated that measurements of the entropy change associated with the addition of electrons to semiconductor- and graphene-based quantum dots accurately quantify the spin and orbital degeneracy of the states into which they are added. However, measuring more exotic entropies requires probing the entropy change of an entire system in response to an added particle. Here, we demonstrate that Maxwell relation-based measurements probe not only the entropy change associated with the added electron but also that of the surrounding system as it responds to that electron. Using a pair of capacitively coupled GaAs quantum dots, we show that charge measurements on one dot reveal entropy changes associated with the entire two-dot system, both at weak dot--reservoir coupling where microstate counting applies and at stronger coupling where numerical renormalization group calculations are required.

</details>

**Summary.** This article reviews the current state and future challenges in cluster science, focusing on the dynamics of electrons in nanoclusters. It explores how external fields and molecular structure can be used to engineer complex quantum phenomena, ranging from chirality-induced spin selectivity to topological phases in synthetic lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** It highlights the use of structured light and microwave dressing to engineer topological properties and tunable interactions in molecular ensembles, which is highly relevant to quantum control and many-body dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

<a id="paper-2605.03663"></a>
### [Enhanced Valley Polarization via Nonlinear Cascaded Quantum-Geometric Selection Rules](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03663v1)

**Authors:** Quentin Courtade, Sotirios Fragkos, Dominique Descamps, Stéphane Petit, Yann Mairesse, Michael Schüler, Samuel Beaulieu  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03663v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03663_figures/2605.03663_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Scheme of the Experimental Setup and Concept of Enhanced Valley Polarization via Non-</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03663_figures/2605.03663_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2: Nonequilibrium Band Mapping and Ultrafast State-Resolved Dynamics. (a) Normalized</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03663_figures/2605.03663_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Enhanced Valley Polarization in High-Lying States. (a)-(b) Experimentally measured momen-</figcaption>
</figure>

**Summary.** This paper shows that using a nonlinear, two-photon cascaded process in MoTe2 can create much stronger valley polarization in high-energy electronic states than standard single-photon methods. By leveraging a real intermediate state, the researchers found they could effectively 'double' the chiroptical selection rules. This discovery provides a new pathway for controlling valley degrees of freedom in ultrafast valleytronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of a Lindblad master equation to model nonlinear, multi-photon light-matter interactions and the exploration of quantum-geometric selection rules in a driven-dissipative regime are highly relevant to researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand how to enhance valley polarization in high-lying conduction bands of transition-metal dichalcogenides beyond the limits of conventional linear optical response.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that a doubly resonant, cascaded nonlinear pathway via a real intermediate state significantly enhances valley polarization in high-lying states compared to the first conduction band.

**Method.** The study combines time- and angle-resolved extreme-ultraviolet photoemission spectroscopy (trARPES) with a time-dependent Lindblad master-equation formalism and DFT calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is monolayer 2H-MoTe2, modeled as a three-level ladder-type electronic structure (valence band, first conduction band, and a high-lying conduction band) using a Lindblad master equation to account for dynamics and dissipation.

**Key observables.** Energy- and momentum-resolved photoemission intensity, band-resolved local orbital angular momentum (Lz), and valley asymmetry (normalized difference between LCP and RCP pump intensities).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pump wavelength (1.2 eV), XUV probe energy (21.6 eV), carrier lifetimes (ranging from ~47 fs to ~1.4 ps), and peak electric field strength (1.6 MV/cm).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a three-level ladder system and uses a Lindblad formalism to approximate dissipative decay and intervalley scattering.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup and orbital angular momentum textures; Figure 2 presents energy-momentum maps, time-resolved intensities, and Lindblad simulations; Figure 3 displays momentum distribution curves for LCP and RCP pumps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of valley polarization enhancement, describes the experimental trARPES setup and theoretical Lindblad framework, presents the observation of enhanced polarization in high-lying states, and provides a microscopic explanation based on cascaded selection rules.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum geometric properties of Bloch electrons fundamentally govern light-matter interactions and optical selection rules in solids. In semiconducting transition-metal dichalcogenides, circularly polarized excitation near the band edge enables valley-selective interband transitions, providing the basis for valleytronics. While nonlinear optical protocols are being developed to manipulate and probe valley selection rules, they largely rely on band-edge transitions that proceed via virtual intermediate states. Here, we demonstrate a doubly resonant cascaded nonlinear pathway from the valence band to high-lying states, mediated by a real intermediate state whose participation substantially reshapes the valley optical selection rules. Using time- and angle-resolved extreme-ultraviolet photoemission spectroscopy in combination with a time-dependent Lindblad master-equation formalism, we show that this cascaded nonlinear photoexcitation produces a substantially enhanced high-lying valley polarization compared to the conventional linear optical response near the band edge. The extension of the quantum-geometry-based selection rules to the nonlinear regime and high-lying bands offers new perspectives for ultrafast valleytronics and should play a determinant role in strong-field-driven phenomena in quantum materials.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the gauge-field component of spin-orbit coupling is crucial for capturing the true spin dynamics in materials under external electric fields. It shows that neglecting this term leads to an incorrect 'symmetry-frozen' description of spins, whereas including it reveals essential symmetry-breaking effects and spin accumulation.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the gauge-field contribution to the spin-orbit coupling (SOC) Hamiltonian is essential for describing symmetry breaking and spin dynamics under external electromagnetic fields.

**Main result.** The gauge-field term is the primary driver of symmetry breaking in the SOC Hamiltonian; without it, spins remain 'symmetry-frozen,' whereas its inclusion allows for the development of dynamical spin states.

**Method.** Real-time time-dependent density functional theory (rt-TDDFT) using the Quantum ESPRESS and the Kleinman-Bylander scheme to incorporate gauge-field-dependent SOC into pseudopotentials.

**Model / system.** The study examines a 2D Rashba-type square lattice model and real materials including the topological insulator Bi2Se3, the Dirac semimetal ZrSiS, and a 1D trigonal Se chain.

**Key observables.** Spin expectation values (Sx, Sy, Sz), spin texture, spin-projected band structures, and spin accumulation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** External electric field strengths (e.g., 0.5 mV/A and 1.0 mV/A), presence/absence of the gauge-field term, and various crystal symmetries (mirror, glide, and screw-rotational).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The relativistic expansion of the electron Hamiltonian is considered up to the (v/c)^2 order.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates mirror planes and compares spin evolution with and without the gauge-field term; Figure 2 shows the crystal structure of ZrSiS; other figures show spin-resolved 3D band structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of gauge-invariance in SOC, defines the decomposed Hamiltonian, presents rt-TDDFT methodology, applies the framework to specific models and real materials (Bi2Se3, ZrSiS), and concludes with the necessity of the gauge-field term for nonequilibrium dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

When a condensed-matter system is subjected to external electromagnetic fields, the gauge-invariant formulation of physical operators must explicitly incorporate the gauge-field contribution. However, in the context of spin-orbit coupling (SOC), this gauge-field term is often regarded as negligible or merely additive compared to the canonical SOC, which is typically localized near atomic cores. Here, we demonstrate that the symmetry breaking and consequent spin dynamics are governed by the gauge-field term, without which the spins remain symmetry-constrained. We perform real-time time-dependent density functional theory calculations to investigate spin-orbit dynamics, focusing on representative cases with mirror, glide, and screw-rotational symmetry. We demonstrate that when the gauge-field term in the time-dependent Hamiltonian perturbs the symmetry of the canonical term, a dynamical spin state gradually develops during the time evolution, beyond the symmetry-frozen states. We suggest that, for nonequilibrium spin-orbit dynamics, the gauge-invariant formulation of SOC is not only formally required but also quantitatively essential, even for a weak external field.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Authors:** Yue Yu

**Summary.** This paper proposes a method to engineer new spin-splitting symmetries in altermagnets using light or loop-current orders. By inducing odd-parity spin textures, it enables new ways to manipulate spin-polarized currents for spintronic applications. The work also predicts a dissipationless spin Hall effect in driven PT-symmetric magnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing a theoretical framework to induce tunable odd-parity spin splittings in collinear altermagnets using two-color light driving or coupling to loop-current orders.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that two-color light driving or P-odd loop-current orders can create mixed-parity spin textures in altermagnets and induce a nonrelativistic dissipationless anomalous spin Hall conductivity in PT-symmetric magnets.

**Method.** The work employs group-theoretical analysis, Landau theory, and microscopic Floquet theory (using the high-frequency and weak-intensity limits) alongside the Kubo formula for transport properties.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on collinear altermagnets (P,T = +, -) and PT-symmetric magnets (P,T = -, -) on square (D4h) and honeycomb (D6h) lattices, using a two-sublattice Hamiltonian with k-dependent hopping and exchange interactions.

**Key observables.** Momentum-dependent spin splitting (even and odd parity), spin-polarized currents, and dissipationless anomalous spin Hall conductivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Two-color light driving with a 2:1 frequency ratio, relative phase between light components, high-frequency limit (omega >> bandwidth), and weak light intensity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the high-frequency limit, small driving amplitude (A << 1), and collinear spin ordering along the z-axis.

**Figures summary.** Figures show Fermi surface evolution (undriven vs. driven), the honeycomb lattice structure for PT-symmetric magnets, and different types of loop-current orders in square lattices.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spin-splitting symmetry, develops a theoretical framework using group theory and Floquet theory, analyzes the response of altermagnets and PT-symmetric magnets to light and loop-currents, and concludes with transport property calculations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Momentum-dependent spin splitting and its relation to inversion ($P$) and time-reversal ($T$) symmetries are central to nonrelativistic spintronics. Representative examples include collinear altermagnets with $(P,T)=(+,-)$ and non-collinear odd-parity magnets with $(P,T)=(-,+)$. In this work, we develop a theoretical framework to induce odd-parity spin splittings in the more abundant collinear altermagnets through two mechanisms: driving by a two-color linearly polarized light field or coupling to a $P$-odd loop-current order. Properly phase-locked two-color driving induces a static $(P,T)=(-,-)$ order, symmetry-equivalent to a translationally invariant $P$-odd loop-current order. Coupling this order to an altermagnet produces a controllable mixed-parity spin texture, opening new avenues for the electrical and optical manipulation of spin-polarized currents in spintronics applications. The same mechanism applied to a collinear $PT$-symmetric magnet induces a distinct $(P,T)=(+,+)$ state with a nonrelativistic dissipationless anomalous spin Hall conductivity. We present group-theory and microscopic Floquet theory to highlight the emergent responses.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03480"></a>
### [Influence of twist angle on ultrafast charge separation in WS2-graphene heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03480v1)

**Authors:** Niklas Hofmann, Leonard Weigl, Johannes Gradl, Stiven Forti, Domenica Convertino, Camilla Coletti, Isabella Gierz  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03480v1>

<img src="2605.03480_figures/2605.03480_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the twist angle is a critical control parameter for charge separation in WS2-graphene heterostructures. Using trARPES, the authors show that while 0-degree alignment enables efficient separation, a 30-degree misalignment leads to simultaneous electron and hole transfer. These findings provide essential insights for engineering next-generation optoelectronic and photovoltaic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates how the twist angle between layers in van der Waals heterostructures influences the efficiency of ultrafast charge separation.

**Main result.** At a 0 degree twist angle, efficient charge separation is observed, whereas at 30 degrees, electron and hole transfer occur at similar rates, preventing separation. The electron transfer rate itself appears independent of the twist angle, potentially due to tunneling through sulfur vacancies.

**Method.** The researchers used time- and angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (trARPES) to probe population dynamics and band shifts following photoexcitation.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of epitaxially grown WS2-graphene heterostructures with twist angles of 0 and 30 degrees, where WS2 islands are grown via CVD on hydrogen-intercalated epitaxial graphene on SiC(0001).

**Key observables.** Population dynamics (gain/loss in graphene Dirac cone and WS2 bands), transient band gap renormalization, and transient band shifts (upward/downward shifts).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Twist angles of 0 and 30 degrees; pump photon energy of 3.1 eV; temporal resolution of 200 fs; energy resolution of 350 meV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Fitting of WS2 valence bands assumed fixed energy differences between spin-split bands and fixed spectral weights based on equilibrium data.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows LEED data for twist angle confirmation; Fig 2 displays equilibrium and pump-induced ARPES spectra; Fig 3 shows population dynamics and Dirac cone changes; Fig 4 tracks evolution of band positions and charging shifts; Figs 5-7 detail the methodology for extracting band positions from EDCs and MDCs.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with structural characterization via LEED, moves to the presentation of trARPES spectral snapshots, analyzes population dynamics and band shifts for both twist angles, compares results to theoretical energy barrier models, and concludes with a discussion on the mechanism of charge transfer.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Van der Waals (vdW) heterostructures, formed by stacking two-dimensional materials, offer highly tunable electronic and optical properties, with the twist angle between layers acting as a critical tuning parameter. While its impact on moiré patterns, band structure, and correlated states is well-established, the influence of twist angle on ultrafast charge transfer remains controversial. Here, we employ time- and angle-resolved photoemission spectroscopy (trARPES) to directly probe ultrafast charge transfer in epitaxially grown WS\textsubscript{2}-graphene heterostructures with twist angles of 0$^{\circ}$ and 30$^{\circ}$. Upon photoexcitation at $\hbarω= 3.1\,\mathrm{eV}$, we observe efficient charge separation at 0$^{\circ}$, while at 30$^{\circ}$, electron and hole transfer occur at similar rates. Our results highlight the crucial role of the twist angle in controlling charge separation efficiency, offering valuable insights for designing vdW heterostructures for applications in photovoltaics and optoelectronics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.03133"></a>
### [Quantum Geometric Quadrupole of Cooper Pairs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03133v1)

**Authors:** Wenqin Chen, Kaijie Yang, Ting Cao, Shi-Zeng Lin, Jiabin Yu, Di Xiao  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03133v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03133_figures/2605.03133_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Semiclassical picture of a two-body bound state in Bloch bands. Under an attractive interaction V , two electrons orbit each other with velocity vF + vA, where the anomalous velocity vA = ∇V × Ωarises from Berry curvature Ω. The separation d between two electron centers sets the size of the circular trajectory, while each electron’s intrinsic spread is characterized by the quantum metric gµν. Together, these two contributions determine the Cooper pair size.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03133_figures/2605.03133_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Cooper pair size in the two-band model of rhombohedral graphene. (a) Schematic of the two-band model formed by the two surface orbitals under a displacement field. (b) Low-energy band dispersion for the potential difference ∆= 80 meV, which is tunable by the displacement field. (c) Fermi level and Fermi momentum as functions of the electron density. (d) Phase vortex structure (color) of the envelope function and the pair geometric dipole dG (arrows). (e) Cooper pair size and its contributions as functions of the winding number N, which is varied as a model parameter. Here kC = 0.3 nm−1. (f) Cooper pair size and its geometric lower bound as functions of the momentum cutoff kC. Here N...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper develops a new framework to calculate the size of Cooper pairs in flat-band superconductors by analyzing the quantum geometric quadrupole moment. It reveals that Berry curvature and the quantum metric impose a fundamental lower bound on the pair size, which can dominate the coherence length in systems like rhombohedral graphene. This identifies quantum geometry as a central driver of the microscopic length scales in correlated electronic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep connection between topological invariants (Berry curvature/metric) and the microscopic spatial structure of many-body bound states, which is relevant to understanding the stability and scale of many-body correlations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the fundamental length scale (size) of Cooper pairs in flat-band superconductors where conventional BCS theory fails due to quenched band dispersion.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the Cooper pair size is determined by a geometric lower bound arising from the quantum metric and Berry curvature, with the latter providing a significant contribution when time-revanl symmetry is broken.

**Method.** A momentum-space derivation using a band-projected BCS framework, decomposing the pair quadrupole moment into amplitude, quantum metric, and phase structure components.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to (nearly) flat-band systems, specifically focusing on rhombohedral graphene and twisted moiré systems using a two-band effective Hamiltonian.

**Key observables.** Cooper pair size (trace of the quadrupole moment), pair Berry curvature, pair quantum metric, and pair geometric dipole.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Berry curvature, quantum metric, winding number, momentum cutoff, and the presence/absence of time-reversal symmetry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes a band-projected BCS framework for intraband pairing, neglects band dispersion in the limit of local attractive interaction, and utilizes a tight-binding approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a semiclassical illustration of the two-body bound state and the roles of anomalous velocity and intrinsic spread; Figure 2 shows the two-band model for rhombohedral graphene and its low-energy dispersion.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of pair size in flat bands, develops a formal mathematical framework for the pair quadrupole moment, decomposes the pair size into three gauge-invariant components, establishes a geometric lower bound, and validates the theory using rhombohedral graphene and the 'Berry trashcan' model.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The size of Cooper pairs defines a fundamental length scale of superconductivity, conventionally set by band dispersion and the superconducting gap. This picture breaks down in flat bands, where quenched dispersion makes quantum geometry essential. Here we develop a general framework based on the Cooper pair quadrupole moment, whose trace gives the pair size. The framework holds for both dispersive and flat-band cases, and provides a unified description of the geometric origin of this length scale. In particular, when time-reversal symmetry is broken, Berry curvature enters through the phase structure of the pair wavefunction and gives an essential contribution absent from previous quantum-metric theories. Together, Berry curvature and quantum metric impose a geometric lower bound on the pair size. Applying this framework to rhombohedral graphene, we find that the Berry-curvature-induced contribution can dominate and yields pair sizes comparable to experimentally inferred coherence lengths. These results identify Berry curvature as a central geometric ingredient controlling the microscopic length scale of superconductivity.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that superconducting islands on suspended nanowires experience a thermal bottleneck where phonons cannot efficiently cool to the bath temperature. By applying a three-temperature model, the authors show that heat is primarily dissipated through the surrounding 3He environment rather than through the nanowire itself.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates the heat balance and thermal relaxation in superconducting islands on freely suspended InAs nanowires, specifically addressing why the standard two-temperature model fails to describe the observed thermal dynamics.

**Main result.** The researchers identified a thermal relaxation bottleneck where phonon heating occurs because the S-island's phonon temperature exceeds the bath temperature, a phenomenon best described by a three-temperature model (3TM) involving environmental cooling via a 3He wetting layer.

**Method.** The study uses Joule spectroscopy to trace the superconductor-to-normal transition and Johnson noise thermometry to calibrate temperatures, while using mesoscopic heaters to vary the effective bath temperature.

**Model / system.** The system consists of epitaxial Al/InAs nanowires with an Al shell, freely suspended above a substrate and immersed in a 3He environment. The thermal dynamics are analyzed using a Three-Temperature Model (3TM) that accounts for electron, phonon, and environmental temperature gradients.

**Key observables.** Critical Joule power (Pj_c), differential resistance (R_diff), noise temperature (T_N), and superconducting critical temperature (T_c) as a function of magnetic field.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Al shell thickness (42 nm), InAs core diameter (~150 nm), electron-phonon coupling strength (Sigma_e-ph), and environmental thermal conductance (Sigma_env).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the existence of a 3He wetting layer for cooling; assumes the phonon heat flux through the nanowire core is negligible compared to environmental cooling.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the device layout, heater arrangement, and I-V curves demonstrating the S-N transition. Supplemental figures include SEM images, magnetic field-dependent resistance plots, and plots showing the non-steady-state behavior of critical power.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the thermal bottleneck problem, describes the experimental platform and Joule spectroscopy method, presents the failure of the 2TM and the success of the 3TM, analyzes the cooling mechanism via 3He, and concludes with a discussion on the wetting layer and non-steady-state effects.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the heat balance in superconducting islands (S-islands) formed in epitaxial Al/InAs nanowires (NWs) freely suspended above the substrate. We employ a Joule spectroscopy approach, which traces the superconductor-normal transition in the S-island mediated by heating of the neighboring InAs NW segments via transport current. The temperature of the surrounding 3He bath is varied with nearby mesoscopic heaters and controlled with the NW Johnson noise thermometry. The experiment reveals a substantial thermal relaxation bottleneck associated with the cooling via surrounding 3He, which gives rise to phonon heating in the S-island. Our results uncover the role of environmental cooling in non-equilibrium experiments in S-islands in NW devices.

</details>

### [A Correction Method for Crack Area Overestimation in Phase-Field Fracture](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03731v1)

**Authors:** M. Castillón, J. Segurado, I. Romero  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03731v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

(12) and (13): the phase-field term, Γϕ, and the gradient term, Γ∇ϕ. Substituting the analytical solution into these definitions yields:</figcaption>
</figure>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new method to correct the overestimation of crack area in phase-field fracture simulations. By leveraging the stability of the gradient energy term, the proposed method provides a mesh-independent way to recover physical fracture properties and eliminate artificial force peaks. It is highly effective for complex 3D geometries where traditional correction methods fail.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Phase-field fracture models suffer from an overestimation of crack area due to numerical artifacts like strain localization, where the phase-field variable artificially saturates across finite elements.

**Main result.** The authors propose the Double Gradient Correction Method (DGCM), which uses the principle of energy equipartition to accurately approximate the true crack area as twice the gradient-dependent energy, effectively suppressing non-physical peak force overshoots.

**Method.** A novel a posteriori correction framework (DGCM) based on the energy equipartition principle, where the crack area is calculated from the gradient term of the energy functional, which is less sensitive to mesh-induced saturation.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes phase-field fracture models, specifically the AT2 (and AT1) regularization formulations, applied to 1D, 2D, and 3D mechanical specimens such as center-cracked tension and compact tension specimens.

**Key observables.** Crack surface area, peak force during crack initiation, force-displacement response, stiffness, and energy release rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phase-field length-scale parameter (l), mesh size (h), critical energy release rate (Gc), and the mesh resolution ratio (l/h).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes the principle of energy equipartition holds (valid as l approaches zero) and that the phase-field profile is sufficiently developed and free from boundary effects.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the transition from sharp to diffuse cracks, energy component convergence, the distortion of phase-field profiles due to localization, and validation of the DGCM against LEFM and other correction methods in 2D and 3D.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the problem of crack area overestimation, introduces the mathematical principle of energy equipartition, derives the DGCM framework, validates it with 1D analytical benchmarks, compares it against existing mesh-based and skeletonization methods, and demonstrates its robustness in complex 2D and 3D simulations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Phase-field fracture models are known to overestimate the crack area, a discrepancy that compromises the accuracy of fracture predictions. This issue stems from the diffuse crack representation and numerical artifacts, such as strain localization, where the phase-field variable artificially saturates across finite elements.   Existing correction strategies, including mesh-dependent factors and skeletonization algorithms, have significant limitations. Mesh-based corrections are often unreliable for unstructured meshes, while skeletonization can be complex and inaccurate for intricate crack topologies, especially in three dimensions.   This paper introduces a novel and robust framework to correct this overestimation. Our approach is founded on the principle of energy equipartition, where the energy contributions from the phase-field and its gradient are equal as the length-scale parameter approaches zero. Since numerical artifacts primarily affect the phase-field term while leaving the gradient term largely unperturbed, we propose that the crack area can be accurately approximated as twice the gradient-dependent energy. This method is inherently mesh-independent and readily applicable to the entire domain, including 3D simulations.   The proposed methodology is validated against benchmarks with analytical solutions and compared with established methods like skeletonization to demonstrate its accuracy. It is then applied to complex geometries with curvilinear crack paths and evaluated in a three-dimensional simulation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Adjacent Sink Strengths Used in Multiscale Kinetic Rate Equation Simulations of Defects and Impurities in Solids](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.03529v1)

**Authors:** Tommy Ahlgren  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03529v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new 'adjacent sink strength' formulation for Kinetic Rate Equation simulations to account for the high probability of defects being retrapped immediately after detrapping. By incorporating this effect, the model corrects significant errors in predicting thermal desorption peaks and ensures that fitted kinetic parameters remain physically consistent. This provides a more accurate multiscale bridge between atomistic simulations and experimental observations in materials science.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional Kinetic Rate Equation (kRE) models fail to capture the enhanced retrapping of defects/impurities released adjacent to traps, leading to systematic errors in predicting Thermal Desorption Spectroscopy (TDS) peak temperatures and physically inconsistent kinetic parameters.

**Main result.** The derivation of analytical expressions for 'adjacent sink strength' (Ka) allows kRE simulations to achieve quantitative agreement with kinetic Monte Carlo benchmarks and prevents the use of non-physical parameters when fitting experimental data.

**Method.** The author derives analytical expressions for adjacent sink strength including jump length corrections and implements a numerical iterative scheme (using Crank-Nicolson and Picard iteration) to solve the coupled nonlinear kREs.

**Model / system.** A mean-field Kinetic Rate Equation (kRE) framework modeling the evolution of defect and impurity concentrations (e.g., hydrogen/helium in solids) subject to diffusion, trapping, and detrapping.

**Key observables.** Thermal Desorption Spectroscopy (TDS) peak temperatures, impurity flux to the surface, and defect concentrations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trap radius (Rt), trap concentration (Ct), diffusion jump length (lambda), trapping energy (Et), detrapping frequency (nu_detr), and the sink strength enhancement factor (epsilon_k).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analytical expressions assume spherical trap geometries, a small jump length limit (lambda/Rt <= 0.5), and a low total trap volume fraction (VF_tot <= 0.1).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares random vs. adjacent sink strengths and shows the enhancement factor; Figure 2 shows TDS peaks with and without adjacent sink strengths; Figure 3 shows TDS peak shifts with concentration and jump length; Figure 6/7 show analytical vs. kMC sink strength comparisons.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the kRE deficiency, derivation of the adjacent sink strength and jump length corrections, numerical implementation strategy, validation via kMC benchmarking, and discussion of parameter estimation risks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Kinetic Rate Equation (kRE) modeling is widely used to simulate defect and impurity evolution in solids over experimentally relevant time and length scales. However, conventional kRE formulations include only random-position sink strengths, which adequately describe trapping of defects created at random lattice sites but fail to capture the enhanced retrapping of defects released directly adjacent to traps during detrapping or dissociation events. This omission leads to systematic errors, including underestimated thermal desorption (TDS) peak temperatures and incorrect kinetic parameters when fitting to experimental data. In this work, we derive for the first time analytical expressions for the adjacent sink strength, including correction for finite impurity diffusion jump length. We provide a practical implementation strategy for integrating these expressions into kRE simulations. Comparisons with kinetic Monte Carlo (kMC) benchmarks demonstrate that adjacent sink strengths dominate the retrapping probability and are essential for reproducing the correct temperature dependence of TDS release peaks. Simulations that employ only random sink strengths can still be tuned to match TDS spectra; however, the resulting fitted trapping energies, detrapping frequencies, and diffusion parameters are often physically inconsistent. The adjacent sink strength formulation introduced here significantly improves the predictive capability of kRE modeling, enabling accurate multiscale simulations of defect and impurity behavior in materials. This framework also establishes a foundation for future extensions, including adjacent sink strengths associated with extended defects such as dislocations and grain boundaries, offering new opportunities to resolve persistent discrepancies between experimental and simulated trapping energetics.

</details>

**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.03673v1>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.03673_figures/2605.03673_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2. XPS spectra of BFCO and BMCO film: elemental scans for BFCO: (a) Bi 4f, (c) Fe 2p, (e) Cr 2p, and (g) O 1s; elemental scans for BMCO: (b) Bi 4f, (d) Mn 2p, (f) Cr 2p, and (h) O 1s. From the EDS data, it was found that both the films have excess bismuth. While BFCO has near</figcaption>
</figure>

**Summary.** This paper investigates bismuth-based oxide double-perovskites as a non-toxic alternative to lead-based perovskites for solar cells. The researchers achieved visible-light absorption and demonstrated that while current efficiencies are modest, significant improvements are possible through defect engineering. The study provides a roadmap for optimizing these materials for efficient photovoltaics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the toxicity and instability of lead-halide perovskites by exploring bismuth-based oxide double-perovskites (Bi2FeCrO6 and Bi2MnCrO6) as stable, non-toxic alternatives for photovoltaics.

**Main result.** Demonstrated high optical absorption in the visible range and achieved power conversion efficiencies of 1.07% for BFCO and 3.56% for BMCO, with simulations predicting much higher efficiencies if defect density is controlled.

**Method.** Solution-based deposition and thermal annealing were used for synthesis, followed by structural (XRD, SEM, AFM, EDS), electronic (XPS, UPS, Mott-Schottky), and optical (UV-Vis) characterization, alongside SCAPS-1D numerical device simulations.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of bismuth-based oxide double-perovskite thin films (Bi2FeCrO6 and Bi2MnCrO6) with monoclinic P21/c symmetry, integrated into a solar cell architecture: FTO/SnO2/Absorber/Spiro-OMeTAD/Ag.

**Key observables.** Optical absorption coefficient, bandgap energy, band-edge positions (VBM/CBM), carrier density, power conversion efficiency (PCE), and Urbach energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Absorption coefficient (10^4-10^5 cm^-1), carrier density (10^17-10^20 cm^-3), and defect density (Nt).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The numerical simulations assume that reducing defect density to approximately 10^13 cm^-3 can significantly boost efficiency.

**Figures summary.** The notes mention a schematic of the device architecture (Fig. 5b).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of lead toxicity, describes the synthesis and structural characterization of the bismuth-based oxides, presents optoelectronic and device performance results, and concludes with numerical simulations predicting future optimization potential.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Lead poisoning and notorious ambient instability in lead-based halide perovskites pave the way for the exploration of alternative materials for affordable and efficient solar cell fabrication. An important prerequisite to this end is the optoelectronic evaluation of the proposed material. Here we report, optoelectronic characterization of Bi2FeCrO6 (BFCO) and Bi2MnCrO6 (BMCO) thin films vis-à-vis performance of photovoltaic cells. Solution-deposited thin films (350-450 nm) of the above compositions demonstrate a double-perovskite structure with monoclinic P21/c symmetry, albeit with mixed cation valences and deep-level defects. A thorough optoelectronic evaluation exhibits large optical absorption in the visible range (α ~ 104 -105 cm-1), and high carrier density, ~1017-20 cm-3. Ultraviolet photoelectron spectroscopy measurement allowed determination of the positions of the band-edges (valence band maximum and conduction band minimum), required for the selection of carrier transport layers. In its first, BMCO-based FTO/SnO2/BMCO/Spiro-OMeTAD/Ag solar cell produced a maximum 3.56% conversion efficiency. Using numerical simulation, we predict that with suitable defect control, the above conversion efficiency can increase significantly.

</details>

**Authors:** Seong-Hoon Jang, Yiwen Yao, Chuanyu Liu, Linda Zhang, Di Zhang, Xue Jia, Hung Ba Tran, Eric Jianfeng Cheng, Ryuhei Sato, Yusuke Ohashi, Toyoto Sato, Yusuke Hashimoto, Mark Allendorf, Nongnuch Artrith, Marcello Baricco, Andreas Borgschulte, Darren P. **Summary.** This paper proposes a new paradigm for materials discovery that moves beyond simple data-driven AI by embedding physical laws and experimental feedback into a closed-loop ecosystem.
